![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Trump%2C_Putin_Alaska_Arrival_%289260678%29.jpg/250px-Trump%2C_Putin_Alaska_Arrival_%289260678%29.jpg)

Trump and Putin in Anchorage on August 15, 2025, during the Russian-United States Summit.

The [United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States "United States") and [Russia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russia "Russia") maintain strategic foreign relations since the establishment in 1991, a continuation of the relationship the United States has had with various Russian governments since 1803.[^4] While both nations have shared interests in [nuclear safety and security](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear_safety_and_security "Nuclear safety and security"), [nonproliferation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear_proliferation "Nuclear proliferation"), [counterterrorism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Counterterrorism "Counterterrorism"), and [space exploration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_exploration "Space exploration"), their relationship has been shown through a mixture of cooperation, competition, and hostility, with both countries considering one another foreign adversaries for much of their relationship. Since the beginning of the [second Trump administration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Second_Trump_administration "Second Trump administration"), the US has pursued normalization and the bettering of relations, largely centered around the resolution of the [Russian invasion of Ukraine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_invasion_of_Ukraine "Russian invasion of Ukraine").

After the [dissolution of the Soviet Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Soviet_Union "Dissolution of the Soviet Union") in 1991 and the end of the [Cold War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War "Cold War"), the relationship was generally warm under Russian president [Boris Yeltsin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boris_Yeltsin "Boris Yeltsin") (1991–99). In the early years of Yeltsin's presidency, the United States and Russia established a cooperative relationship and worked closely together to address global issues such as [arms control](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arms_control "Arms control"), counterterrorism, and the [conflict in Bosnia and Herzegovina](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bosnian_War "Bosnian War"). During Yeltsin's second term, United States–Russia relations became more strained. The [NATO intervention in Yugoslavia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yugoslav_Wars "Yugoslav Wars"), in particular, the 1999 [NATO intervention in Kosovo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NATO_intervention_in_Kosovo "NATO intervention in Kosovo"), was [strongly opposed by Yeltsin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boris_Yeltsin#1996%E2%80%931999:_Second_term "Boris Yeltsin").[^5] [^6] [^7] [^8] Although the Soviet Union had been strongly opposed by the [Titovian flavour of independence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Josip_Broz_Tito#Tito%E2%80%93Stalin_split "Josip Broz Tito"), Yeltsin saw it as an infringement on Russia's latter-day sphere of influence. [Yeltsin also criticized](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boris_Yeltsin#Relations_with_NATO "Boris Yeltsin") NATO's [expansion into Eastern Europe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Enlargement_of_NATO "Enlargement of NATO"), which he saw as a threat to Russia's security.[^9]

After [Vladimir Putin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Putin "Vladimir Putin") became President of Russia in 2000, he initially sought to improve relations with the United States. The two countries cooperated on issues such as counterterrorism and arms control. Putin worked closely with United States president [George W. Bush](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_W._Bush "George W. Bush") on the [war in Afghanistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_\(2001%E2%80%932021\) "War in Afghanistan (2001–2021)") following the [9/11 attacks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/9/11_attacks "9/11 attacks"). Following Putin's [re-election to the Russian presidency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russia_under_Vladimir_Putin "Russia under Vladimir Putin") in 2012, relations between the two countries were significantly strained due to Russia's [annexation of Crimea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Annexation_of_Crimea_by_the_Russian_Federation "Annexation of Crimea by the Russian Federation") and the [Russian military intervention in Ukraine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_military_intervention_in_Ukraine_\(2014%E2%80%93present\) "Russian military intervention in Ukraine (2014–present)"). Deterioration continued with the [Russian military intervention](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_military_intervention_in_the_Syrian_Civil_War "Russian military intervention in the Syrian Civil War") in the [Syrian civil war](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Syrian_civil_war "Syrian civil war").

Relations further deteriorated during the [presidency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Presidency_of_Joe_Biden "Presidency of Joe Biden") of [Joe Biden](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joe_Biden "Joe Biden") following the [Russian invasion of Ukraine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_invasion_of_Ukraine "Russian invasion of Ukraine") in 2022.[^10] [International sanctions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_sanctions_during_the_Russo-Ukrainian_War "International sanctions during the Russo-Ukrainian War") imposed since 2014 were significantly expanded by the U.S. and its allies, including several state-owned banks and oligarchs.[^11] During the second presidency of [Donald Trump](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Donald_Trump "Donald Trump"), the United States has attempted to normalize relations with Russia and has sided with Russia in the United Nations, voting against a resolution to condemn Russia's invasion of Ukraine in February 2025, in a dramatic departure from the long-standing American position on humanitarian issues since 2014.[^12] Defense Secretary [Pete Hegseth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pete_Hegseth "Pete Hegseth") has also ordered the suspension of offensive cyber operations against Russia. Despite this, relations between the two countries are still poor due to the war in Ukraine, and further due to the [Iran war](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2026_Iran_war "2026 Iran war") in 2026.

## Background

### United States and the Russian Empire

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/View_of_Settlement_Ross%2C_1841_%28variation%29.jpg/250px-View_of_Settlement_Ross%2C_1841_%28variation%29.jpg)

Fort Ross, Russian settlement in California, 1841, by Ilya Gavrilovich Voznesensky.

Official contacts between the [Russian Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_Empire "Russian Empire") and the new United States began in 1776. Russia, while formally neutral during the [American Revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Revolution "American Revolution") (1765–1783), favored the U.S.[^13]

There was little trade or migration before the late 19th century. Formal diplomatic ties were established in 1809.[^14] During the [American Civil War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Civil_War "American Civil War"), Russia supported [the Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Union_\(American_Civil_War\) "Union (American Civil War)"), largely because it believed that the U.S. served as a counterbalance to its geopolitical rival, the United Kingdom. In 1863, the [Russian Navy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Imperial_Russian_Navy "Imperial Russian Navy") 's Baltic and Pacific fleets wintered in the American ports of New York and San Francisco, respectively.[^15]

Russia operated a small fur-trade operations in [Alaska](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Alaska "History of Alaska"), coupled with missionaries to the natives. By 1861, the project had lost money, threatened to antagonize the Americans, and could not be defended from Britain. It proved practically impossible to entice Russians to permanently migrate to Alaska; only a few hundred were there in 1867. In the [Alaska Purchase](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alaska_Purchase "Alaska Purchase") of 1867, the land was sold to the United States for $7.2 million.[^16] [^17]

The Russian administrators and military left Alaska, but some missionaries stayed on to minister to the many natives who converted to the [Russian Orthodox](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orthodox_Church_in_America "Orthodox Church in America") faith.[^18]

After 1880, repeated anti-Jewish [pogroms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pogroms "Pogroms") in Russia alienated American elite and public opinion. In 1903, the [Kishinev pogrom](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chi%C8%99in%C4%83u_pogrom_\(1903\) "Chișinău pogrom (1903)") killed 47 Jews, injured 400, and left 10,000 homeless and dependent on relief. American Jews began large-scale organized financial help and assisted in emigration.[^19]

The [Treaty of Portsmouth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Treaty_of_Portsmouth "Treaty of Portsmouth") (1905), brokered by United States president [Theodore Roosevelt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt "Theodore Roosevelt"), ended the [Russo-Japanese War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russo-Japanese_War "Russo-Japanese War").[^20]

During [World War I](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_War_I "World War I"), the [United States declaration of war on Germany (1917)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_declaration_of_war_on_Germany_\(1917\) "United States declaration of war on Germany (1917)") came after [Nicholas II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nicholas_II "Nicholas II") had abdicated as a result of the [February Revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/February_Revolution "February Revolution"). When the tsar was still in power, many Americans deplored fighting a war with him as an ally. With him gone, the [Wilson administration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wilson_administration "Wilson administration") used the new provisional government to describe how the democratic nations were fighting against autocratic old empires of [Germany](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/German_Empire "German Empire") and [Austria-Hungary](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Austria-Hungary "Austria-Hungary"). During the war, the [American Expeditionary Forces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Expeditionary_Forces "American Expeditionary Forces") were just starting to see battle when the [October Revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/October_Revolution "October Revolution") happened in which the [Bolsheviks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bolsheviks "Bolsheviks") overthrew the provisional government and removed Russia from the war.

Before the armistice in November 1918, the Americans had helped the [Allied intervention in the Russian Civil War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Allied_intervention_in_the_Russian_Civil_War "Allied intervention in the Russian Civil War") with the [Polar Bear Expedition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polar_Bear_Expedition "Polar Bear Expedition") and the [American Expeditionary Force Siberia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Expeditionary_Force_Siberia "American Expeditionary Force Siberia"). The Americans' goal was not necessarily ideological but rather to prevent the German enemy from gaining access to war supplies controlled by the Bolsheviks, though the United States also tacitly supported the [White movement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/White_movement "White movement") against the Bolsheviks.[^21]

From 1820 until 1917, about 3.3 million [immigrants arrived in the U.S.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Immigration_to_the_United_States "Immigration to the United States") from the Russian Empire. Most were Jews, Poles, or Lithuanians; only 100,000 were ethnic Russians.[^22] [^23]

### United States and the Soviet Union

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Teheran_conference-1943.jpg/330px-Teheran_conference-1943.jpg)

Soviet general secretary Joseph Stalin, U.S. president Franklin D. Roosevelt, and UK prime minister Winston Churchill in Tehran, Iran in November 1943.

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/President_Ronald_Reagan_and_Vice-President_Bush_Meet_with_Soviet_General_Secretary_Gorbachev_on_Governor%27S_Island_New_York_-_DPLA_-_538568cce6b35aed223fca913da4ea7b.jpg/330px-thumbnail.jpg)

U.S. vice president George H. W. Bush and President Ronald Reagan with Soviet general secretary Mikhail Gorbachev in New York, 1988.

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Life_expectancy_in_Russia_and_the_US.png/330px-Life_expectancy_in_Russia_and_the_US.png)

Comparison of life expectancy in the US and Russia since 1960

By 1921, after the [Bolsheviks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bolsheviks "Bolsheviks") gained the upper hand in the [Russian Civil War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_Civil_War "Russian Civil War"), [executed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Execution_of_the_Romanov_family "Execution of the Romanov family") the [Romanov imperial family](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/House_of_Romanov "House of Romanov"), repudiated the [tsarist debt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Repudiation_of_debt_at_the_Russian_Revolution "Repudiation of debt at the Russian Revolution"), and called for a world revolution by the working class, it was regarded as a [pariah nation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rogue_state "Rogue state") by most of the world.[^24] Beyond the Russian Civil War, relations were also dogged by claims of American companies for compensation for the [nationalized industries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nationalization "Nationalization") they had invested in.[^25] The U.S., while starting to develop trade and economic ties, was the last major world power that continued to refuse to formally recognize the Soviet government.[^26] The United States and Soviet Russia established diplomatic relations in November 1933.[^27]

The United States and the Soviet Union, along with Britain, were the leaders of the [Allies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II "Allies of World War II") against the [Axis powers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Axis_powers "Axis powers") during [World War II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_War_II "World War II"). Following the onset of the [Cold War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War "Cold War") in 1947, the [North Atlantic Treaty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_Atlantic_Treaty "North Atlantic Treaty") was signed by the U.S., Canada, and several Western European nations, on April 4, 1949, a treaty that established the [North Atlantic Treaty Organization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NATO "NATO") (NATO) designed to provide collective security against the Soviet Union.[^28]

The first bilateral treaty between the U.S. and Soviet Russia/USSR was a [consular](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Consular "Consular") convention signed in Moscow in June 1964.[^29] [^30] In 1975, the [Helsinki Final Act](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Helsinki_Accords "Helsinki Accords") was signed by a multitude of countries, including the USSR and the US, and, while not having a binding legal power of a treaty, it effectively signified the U.S.-led West's recognition of the Soviet Union's dominance in [Eastern Europe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eastern_Europe "Eastern Europe") and acceptance of the [Soviet annexation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Occupation_of_the_Baltic_states "Occupation of the Baltic states") of [Estonia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Estonia "Estonia"), [Latvia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Latvia "Latvia") and [Lithuania](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lithuania "Lithuania") that had been effected in 1940. The Act came to play a role in subsequently ending the Cold War.[^31]

In the 1970s—1980s, the USSR and the U.S. signed a series of [arms control](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arms_control "Arms control") treaties such as the [Anti-Ballistic Missile Treaty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anti-Ballistic_Missile_Treaty "Anti-Ballistic Missile Treaty") (1972), two [Strategic Arms Limitation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Strategic_Arms_Limitation_Talks "Strategic Arms Limitation Talks") treaties (SALT), the [Intermediate-Range Nuclear Forces Treaty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intermediate-Range_Nuclear_Forces_Treaty "Intermediate-Range Nuclear Forces Treaty") (1987); in July 1991 the [Strategic Arms Reduction Treaty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/START_I "START I") was concluded.

In the late 1980s, [Eastern Europe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eastern_Europe "Eastern Europe") nations took advantage of the relaxation of [Soviet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union "Soviet Union") control under [General Secretary](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/General_Secretary_of_the_Communist_Party_of_the_Soviet_Union "General Secretary of the Communist Party of the Soviet Union") [Mikhail Gorbachev](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mikhail_Gorbachev "Mikhail Gorbachev") and began to [break away from communist rule](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Revolutions_of_1989 "Revolutions of 1989"). The relationship greatly improved in the final years of the USSR.

On December 3, 1989, Soviet general secretary Gorbachev and U.S. president [George H. W. Bush](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_H._W._Bush "George H. W. Bush") declared the [Cold War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War "Cold War") over at the [Malta Summit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Malta_Summit "Malta Summit").[^32]

Both countries agreed to cut their strategic nuclear weapons by 30 percent, and the Soviet Union promised to reduce its [intercontinental ballistic missile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intercontinental_ballistic_missile "Intercontinental ballistic missile") force by 50 percent.[^33] In August 1991, hard-line Communists launched a [coup](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1991_Soviet_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat_attempt "1991 Soviet coup d'état attempt") against Gorbachev; while the coup quickly fell apart, it broke the remaining power of Gorbachev and the central Soviet government.[^34] Later that month, Gorbachev resigned as [general secretary of the Communist party](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/General_Secretary_of_the_Communist_Party_of_the_Soviet_Union "General Secretary of the Communist Party of the Soviet Union"), but remained [president of the Soviet Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/President_of_the_Soviet_Union "President of the Soviet Union"), and Russian president [Boris Yeltsin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boris_Yeltsin "Boris Yeltsin") ordered the seizure of Soviet property. Gorbachev clung to power as the president of the Soviet Union until December 25, 1991, when the Soviet Union [dissolved](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Soviet_Union "Dissolution of the Soviet Union").[^35] [Fifteen states](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Post-Soviet_states "Post-Soviet states") emerged from the Soviet Union, with the largest and most populous one, [Russia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russia "Russia"), taking full responsibility for all the rights and obligations of the USSR under the Charter of the United Nations, including the financial obligations. As such, Russia assumed the Soviet Union's [UN membership and permanent membership on the Security Council](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russia_and_the_United_Nations "Russia and the United Nations"), nuclear stockpile and the control over the armed forces; Soviet embassies abroad became Russian embassies.[^36] Bush and Yeltsin met in February 1992, declaring a new era of "friendship and partnership".[^37] In January 1993, Bush and Yeltsin agreed to [START II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/START_II "START II"), which provided for further nuclear arms reductions on top of the original START treaty.[^38]

## History

### Dissolution of the Soviet Union through Yeltsin's terms (1991–99)

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Boris_Yeltsin_with_Bill_Clinton-1.jpg/330px-Boris_Yeltsin_with_Bill_Clinton-1.jpg)

Russian president Boris Yeltsin and U.S. president Bill Clinton in the White House, October 1995.

With Communist politicians and parties in Eastern Europe mostly defunct, on December 26, 1991, the Soviet Union [self-dissolved](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transition_period_and_cessation_of_the_existence_of_the_Soviet_Union "Transition period and cessation of the existence of the Soviet Union"), and the [Commonwealth of Independent States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Independent_States "Commonwealth of Independent States"), a loose association was formed on December 8–21 by eventually 12 of the 15 [Soviet constituent Union Republics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Union_Republics_of_the_Soviet_Union "Union Republics of the Soviet Union"), leaving out earlier the three [Baltic states](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Baltic_states "Baltic states").[^39] The [Russian Soviet Federative Socialist Republic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_Soviet_Federative_Socialist_Republic "Russian Soviet Federative Socialist Republic") became the Russian Federation. It was now an independent state that inherited the USSR's [UN Security Council](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council "United Nations Security Council") permanent membership and became the [sole continuator state to the USSR and one of 12 successor states to the USSR](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Succession,_continuity_and_legacy_of_the_Soviet_Union "Succession, continuity and legacy of the Soviet Union").

Security issues have always been among the most important between the U.S. and Russia. Immediately after the signing of the [Agreement establishing the Commonwealth of Independent States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Agreement_establishing_the_Commonwealth_of_Independent_States "Agreement establishing the Commonwealth of Independent States") on December 8, 1991, Russian president Yeltsin called U.S. president [George H. W. Bush](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_H._W._Bush "George H. W. Bush") and specifically read him Article 6 of the Agreement. "First of all, I talked with [USSR Minister of Defense](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/USSR_Minister_of_Defense "USSR Minister of Defense") Shaposhnikov. I want to read the 6th Article of the Agreement. As a matter of fact Shaposhnikov fully agreed and supported our position. I am now reading Article 6."... "Please note well the next paragraph, Mr. President (and I urge the interpreter to translate this precisely)."... "Dear George, I am finished. This is extremely, extremely important. Because of the tradition between us, I couldn't even wait ten minutes to call you." [^40] According to the text of Article 6, Russia, Ukraine and Belarus form a " [common military and strategic space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Military_of_the_Commonwealth_of_Independent_States "Military of the Commonwealth of Independent States") " and " [united armed forces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Armed_Forces_of_the_Commonwealth_of_Independent_States "United Armed Forces of the Commonwealth of Independent States")." [^41] [^42]

Current [US Secretary of State](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/US_Secretary_of_State "US Secretary of State") [James Baker](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/James_Baker "James Baker") stated that no one but Russia could control Soviet nuclear weapons, in particular, making a statement on December 10, 1991, at [Princeton University](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Princeton_University "Princeton University").[^43]

On December 21, 1991, Boris Yeltsin, President of Russia, sent a letter to [NATO](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NATO "NATO") asking it to consider accepting Russia as a member of the alliance sometime in the future. In the letter to NATO, Yeltsin stated, "This would contribute to an atmosphere of mutual understanding and trust and would strengthen stability and cooperation on the European continent. We regard this relationship as serious and wish to develop this dialog on all fronts, both on the political and military levels. Today we raise the issue of Russia's membership in NATO, however, we see this as a long-term political goal." [^44] The [Collective Security Treaty within the framework of the Commonwealth of Independent States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Collective_Security_Treaty_Organization "Collective Security Treaty Organization") was signed On May 15, 1992.[^45] (A separate organization outside the CIS framework called [Collective Security Treaty Organization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Collective_Security_Treaty_Organization "Collective Security Treaty Organization") was created in 2002).

On January 31, 1992, Yeltsin attended a UN Security Council meeting and said: "I think the time has come to consider creating a *global defence system for the world community*. We are ready to participate actively in building and putting in place a *pan-European collective security system* – in particular during the Vienna talks and the upcoming post-Helsinki-II talks on security and cooperation in Europe. Russia regards the United States and the West not as mere partners but rather as allies.[^46]

[Strobe Talbott](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Strobe_Talbott "Strobe Talbott"), who was Washington's chief expert on Russia, has argued that Clinton hit it off with Russian [Boris Yeltsin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boris_Yeltsin "Boris Yeltsin"), the president of Russia 1991–1999:

The personal diplomacy between Clinton and Yeltsin, augmented by the channel that Gore developed with Yeltsin's longest-serving prime minister, [Victor Chernomyrdin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Victor_Chernomyrdin "Victor Chernomyrdin"), yielded half a dozen major understandings that either resolved or alleviated disputes over Russia's role in the post–cold war world. The two presidents were the negotiators in chief of agreements to halt the sale of Russian rocket parts to India; remove Soviet-era nuclear missiles from Ukraine in exchange for Russian assurances of Ukraine's sovereignty and security; withdraw Russian troops from the Baltic states; institutionalize cooperation between Russia and an expanding NATO; lay the ground for the Baltic states to join the alliance; and ensure the participation of the Russian military in Balkan peacekeeping and of Russian diplomacy in the settlement of NATO's air war against Serbia.[^47]

As the collapse of the [Soviet Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union "Soviet Union") appeared imminent, the [United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States "United States") and their [NATO](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NATO "NATO") allies grew concerned of the risk of nuclear weapons held in the [Soviet republics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Republics_of_the_Soviet_Union "Republics of the Soviet Union") falling into enemy hands. The [Cooperative Threat Reduction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cooperative_Threat_Reduction "Cooperative Threat Reduction") (CTR) program was initiated by the [Nunn–Lugar Act](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Nunn%E2%80%93Lugar_Act&action=edit&redlink=1 "Nunn–Lugar Act (page does not exist)") (really the [Soviet Nuclear Threat Reduction Act of 1991](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Nuclear_Threat_Reduction_Act_of_1991 "Soviet Nuclear Threat Reduction Act of 1991")), which was authored and cosponsored by [Sens](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Senate "United States Senate"). [Sam Nunn](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sam_Nunn "Sam Nunn") ([D](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Democratic_Party_\(United_States\) "Democratic Party (United States)") - [GA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georgia_\(U.S._state\) "Georgia (U.S. state)")) and [Richard Lugar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Richard_Lugar "Richard Lugar") ([R](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Republican_Party_\(United_States\) "Republican Party (United States)") - [IN](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indiana "Indiana")). According to the CTR website, the purpose of the CTR Program was originally "to secure and dismantle [weapons of mass destruction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Weapons_of_mass_destruction "Weapons of mass destruction") and their associated infrastructure in former Soviet Union states."

Relations between Yeltsin and the administrations of [George H. W. Bush](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Foreign_policy_of_the_George_H._W._Bush_administration "Foreign policy of the George H. W. Bush administration") (1989–1993) and [Bill Clinton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Foreign_policy_of_the_Bill_Clinton_administration "Foreign policy of the Bill Clinton administration") (1993–2001) started off well, but deteriorated after 1997. Yeltsin and his foreign minister [Andrey Kozyrev](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andrey_Kozyrev "Andrey Kozyrev") made a high priority Russia's full membership into the family of democratic nations. They wanted to be a partner of the United States. At home they tried to create democratic institutions and a free-market capitalist system.

In 1993, both nations signed the [START II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/START_II "START II") arms control treaty that was designed to ban the use of [multiple independently targetable reentry vehicles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Multiple_independently_targetable_reentry_vehicle "Multiple independently targetable reentry vehicle") (MIRVs) on [intercontinental ballistic missiles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intercontinental_ballistic_missile "Intercontinental ballistic missile") (ICBMs). The treaty was eventually ratified by both countries, yet it was never implemented and was formally abandoned in 2002, following the U.S.'s withdrawal from the 1972 [Anti-Ballistic Missile Treaty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anti-Ballistic_Missile_Treaty "Anti-Ballistic Missile Treaty").

Clinton and Yeltsin were personally friendly. Washington encouraged the rapid transition to a liberal capitalist system in Russia.[^48] Clinton provided rich talking points but provided less than $3 billion, and much was paid to American contractors. The Russians—aware of the Marshall Plan in the 1940s—had counted on far larger sums.[^49] A 1995 NATO study on enlarging the alliance, and the 1999 admission of the Czech Republic, Hungary, and Poland into NATO, alarmed Russia.[^50] With the Cold War over, Russians felt NATO's original role was no longer needed. It feared its [dramatic move eastward](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Enlargement_of_NATO#Visegr%C3%A1d_Group "Enlargement of NATO") meant an escalation of NATO's historic role in containment of Russian goals.[^51] [^52] Fears over NATO enlargement contributed to the rise of [Vladimir Putin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Putin "Vladimir Putin") and his emphasis on Russian nationalism and security issues.[^50]

On January 14, 1994, Russian president Boris Yeltsin said at a meeting with his American counterpart [Bill Clinton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bill_Clinton "Bill Clinton") in [Novo-Ogaryovo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Novo-Ogaryovo "Novo-Ogaryovo"). "Russia has to be the first country to join NATO. Then the others from Central and Eastern Europe can come in. There should be a kind of cartel of the U.S., Russia, and the Europeans to help ensure and improve world security." "In truth, Russia is not yet ready to join NATO. Russia firsts needs to start thinking about reactions in other areas. There is a potential Chinese reaction. As a result, without cooperation between the two of us it is hard to envisage continuation of a peaceful and stable world. If we continue to work together as you suggest, we can do much to ensure peace and stability for Europe and for the rest of the world." [^53] [^54] [^55]

In September 1994, Boris Yeltsin addressed the UN General Assembly and mentioned the role of the [CSCE](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conference_on_Security_and_Co-operation_in_Europe "Conference on Security and Co-operation in Europe") in the European security system. Russia had previously proposed the idea of increasing the role of the CSCE to the detriment of NATO. Yeltsin's national security aide Yuri Baturin noted that after the end of the Cold War, 'the time of NATO has passed,' and therefore the alliance 'should change its mechanisms and goals taking into account Russia's military and political weight.' Baturin believes that 'a new mechanism of European security could be born from the combination of the CSCE and NATO, where the CSCE bodies would represent the political and diplomatic part, and NATO bodies would represent the military part.' But Yeltsin himself did not make such a statement.[^56] By 1995 the [Agreement on the creation the Commonwealth of Independent States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Agreement_on_the_creation_the_Commonwealth_of_Independent_States "Agreement on the creation the Commonwealth of Independent States"), including the Article 6 on "common military and strategic space", came into force for all 12 countries.[^57]

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Yeltsin_and_Clinton_20_June_1999.jpg/330px-Yeltsin_and_Clinton_20_June_1999.jpg)

Yeltsin and Clinton at Cologne's Renaissance House, June 1999.

On March 21, 1997, Yeltsin stated to Bill Clinton in Finland: "Our position has not changed. It remains a mistake for NATO to move eastward. But I need to take steps to alleviate the negative consequences of this for Russia. I am prepared to enter into an agreement with NATO not because I want to but because it is a forced step. There is no other solution for today. The principal issues for me are the following. The agreement must be legally binding – signed by all 16 Allies. Decisions by NATO are not to be taken without taking into account the concerns or opinions of Russia. Also, nuclear and conventional arms cannot move eastward into new members to the borders of Russia, thus creating a new cordon sanitaire aimed at Russia. But one thing is very important: enlargement shouldn't embrace the former Soviet republics. I cannot sign any agreement without such language. Especially Ukraine. If you get them involved, it will create difficulties in our talks with Ukraine on a number of issues." [^58] [^59]

Russia stridently opposed the U.S.-led [NATO military operation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NATO_bombing_of_Yugoslavia "NATO bombing of Yugoslavia") against [Serbia and Montenegro](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Serbia_and_Montenegro "Serbia and Montenegro") over [Kosovo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kosovo "Kosovo") that began in March 1999.[^60] [^61] [^62] In December 1999, while on a visit to China, President Yeltsin verbally assailed Clinton for criticizing Russia's tactics in [Chechnya](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chechnya "Chechnya") (at the start of the [Second Chechen War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Second_Chechen_War "Second Chechen War")) emphatically stating that Russia remained a nuclear power.[^63]

### Putin and George W. Bush (2001–2009)

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Vladimir_Putin_in_Saint_Petersburg-56.jpg/330px-Vladimir_Putin_in_Saint_Petersburg-56.jpg)

Russia strongly opposed the U.S.-led 2003 invasion of Iraq.

In 2001, in response to the [terrorist attacks on September 11](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/September_11_attacks "September 11 attacks"), the new Russian president Vladimir Putin quickly announced strong support. Terrorism against Russia was already high on Putin's agenda and he found common ground by supporting the American/ [NATO](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NATO "NATO") invasion of Afghanistan to destroy the [Taliban](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taliban "Taliban") that had harbored the [Al-Qaeda](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al-Qaeda "Al-Qaeda") terrorists.[^64] By 2002, however, the two countries were escalating their disagreements. Russia became more assertive in international affairs; [George W. Bush](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_W._Bush "George W. Bush") took an increasingly unilateral course in [foreign policy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Foreign_policy_of_the_George_W._Bush_administration "Foreign policy of the George W. Bush administration").[^65]

In 2002, the United States withdrew from the [Anti-Ballistic Missile Treaty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anti-Ballistic_Missile_Treaty "Anti-Ballistic Missile Treaty") in order to move forward with plans for a [missile defense](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Missile_defense "Missile defense") system. Putin called the decision a mistake. Russia strongly opposed the 2003 [invasion of Iraq](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Invasion_of_Iraq "Invasion of Iraq"), though without exercising its veto in the [United Nations Security Council](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council "United Nations Security Council"). Russia has regarded the expansion of NATO into the old [Eastern Bloc](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Warsaw_Pact "Warsaw Pact"), and U.S. efforts to gain access to [Central Asian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_Asia "Central Asia") oil and natural gas as a potentially hostile encroachment on Russia's [sphere of influence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sphere_of_influence "Sphere of influence"). The Russian leadership blamed U.S. officials for encouraging anti-Russian revolts during the [Rose Revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rose_Revolution "Rose Revolution") in Georgia in 2003 and the [Orange Revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orange_Revolution "Orange Revolution") in [Ukraine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ukraine "Ukraine") in 2004. Putin saw intrusions into Russia's historic sphere of interest.[^66] [^67]

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/32nd_G8_Summit-3.jpg/330px-32nd_G8_Summit-3.jpg)

Vladimir Putin with George W. Bush and other Western leaders at 32nd G8 summit in Moscow, July 2006.

Russia condemned the unilateral [declaration of independence of Kosovo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2008_Kosovo_declaration_of_independence "2008 Kosovo declaration of independence") from [Serbia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Serbia "Serbia") in February 2008,[^68] stating they "expect the UN mission and NATO-led forces in Kosovo to take immediate action to carry out their mandate \[...\] including the annulling of the decisions of Pristina's self-governing organs and the taking of tough administrative measures against them." [^69] Russian president Putin described the recognition of Kosovo's independence by the United States and other Western countries as "a terrible precedent, which will de facto blow apart the whole system of international relations, developed not over decades, but over centuries", and that "they have not thought through the results of what they are doing. At the end of the day it is a two-ended stick and the second end will come back and hit them in the face".[^70] In March 2014, Russia used Kosovo's declaration of independence as a justification for recognizing [the independence of Crimea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Declaration_of_Independence_of_the_Republic_of_Crimea "Declaration of Independence of the Republic of Crimea"), citing the so-called " [Kosovo independence precedent](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kosovo_independence_precedent "Kosovo independence precedent") ".[^71] [^72]

In early 2008, President [George W. Bush](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_W._Bush "George W. Bush") vowed full support for admitting [Ukraine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ukraine "Ukraine") and [Georgia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georgia_\(country\) "Georgia (country)") into NATO,[^73] despite Russia's opposition to the further [eastward expansion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Enlargement_of_NATO "Enlargement of NATO") of NATO.[^74] Russia's Deputy Foreign Minister [Grigory Karasin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Grigory_Karasin "Grigory Karasin") warned that any incorporation of Ukraine into NATO would cause a "deep crisis" in [Russia–Ukraine relations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russia%E2%80%93Ukraine_relations "Russia–Ukraine relations") and also negatively affect Russia's relations with the West.[^75]

#### Controversy over U.S. plan to station missiles in Poland (2007–2008)

In March 2007, the U.S. announced plans to build an [anti-ballistic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anti-ballistic_missile "Anti-ballistic missile") [missile defense](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Missile_defense "Missile defense") installation in Poland along with a radar station in the [Czech Republic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Czech_Republic "Czech Republic"). Both nations were former [Warsaw Pact](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Warsaw_Pact "Warsaw Pact") members and both had repudiated Communism and Russian interference. U.S. officials said that the system was intended to protect the United States and Europe from possible nuclear missile attacks by Iran or North Korea. Russia, however, viewed the new system as a potential threat and, in response, tested a long-range [intercontinental ballistic missile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intercontinental_ballistic_missile "Intercontinental ballistic missile"), the [RS-24](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RS-24 "RS-24"), which it claimed could defeat any defense system. Putin warned the U.S. that these new tensions could turn Europe into a powder keg. On June 3, 2007, Putin warned that if the United States built the missile defense system, Russia would consider targeting missiles at Poland and the Czech Republic.[^76]

In October 2007, Vladimir Putin visited Iran to discuss Russia's aid to Iran's nuclear power program and "insisted that the use of force was unacceptable." [^77] On October 17, Bush stated "if you're interested in avoiding World War III, it seems like you ought to be interested in preventing them from having the knowledge necessary to make a nuclear weapon," understood as a message to Putin.[^78] A week later, Putin compared U.S. plans to put up a missile defense system near Russia's border as analogous to when the Soviet Union deployed missiles in Cuba, prompting the [Cuban Missile Crisis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cuban_Missile_Crisis "Cuban Missile Crisis").[^79]

In July 2008, Russia announced that if a U.S. anti-missile shield was deployed near the Russian border, it would have to react militarily. The statement from the Russian foreign ministry said, "If an American strategic anti-missile shield starts to be deployed near our borders, we will be forced to react not in a diplomatic fashion but with military-technical means." Later, Russia's ambassador to the United Nations [Vitaly Churkin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vitaly_Churkin "Vitaly Churkin") said that "military-technical means" did not mean military action, but more likely a change in Russia's strategic posture, perhaps by redeploying its own missiles.[^80]

On August 14, 2008, the U.S. and Poland agreed to have 10 two-stage missile interceptors – made by [Orbital Sciences Corporation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbital_Sciences_Corporation "Orbital Sciences Corporation") – placed in Poland, as part of a missile shield to defend Europe and the U.S. from a possible missile attack by Iran. In return, the U.S. agreed to move a battery of [MIM-104 Patriot](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MIM-104_Patriot "MIM-104 Patriot") missiles to Poland. The missile battery was to be staffed – at least temporarily – by U.S. Military personnel. The U.S. also pledged to defend Poland, a [NATO](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NATO "NATO") member, quicker than NATO would in the event of an attack. Additionally, the Czech Republic recently agreed to allow the placement of a radar-tracking station in their country, despite public opinion polls showing that the majority of Czechs were against the plans and only 18% supported it.[^81] The radar-tracking station in the Czech Republic would also be part of the missile defense shield. After the agreement was announced, Russian officials said defences on Russia's borders would be increased and that they foresaw harm in bilateral relations with the United States.[^82]

#### Russian-Georgian clash (August 2008)

In August 2008, United States-Russia bilateral relations became further strained, when Russia and [Georgia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georgia_\(country\) "Georgia (country)") fought a [five-day war](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russo-Georgian_War "Russo-Georgian War") over the Russian-backed self-proclaimed republics of [South Ossetia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Ossetia "South Ossetia") and [Abkhazia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abkhazia "Abkhazia"). President Bush said to Russia, "Bullying and intimidation are not acceptable ways to conduct foreign policy in the 21st century." [^83]

### Obama administration (2009–2017)

#### "Reset" under Obama and Medvedev (2009–11)

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Obama_and_Medvedev_sign_Prague_Treaty_2010.jpeg/330px-Obama_and_Medvedev_sign_Prague_Treaty_2010.jpeg)

U.S. president Barack Obama and Russian president Dmitry Medvedev after signing the New START treaty

Despite U.S.–Russia relations becoming strained during the [Bush administration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_W._Bush_administration "George W. Bush administration"), Russian president [Dmitry Medvedev](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dmitry_Medvedev "Dmitry Medvedev") (president from May 2008 until May 2012, with [Vladimir Putin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Putin "Vladimir Putin") as Prime Minister during this period) and U.S. president [Barack Obama](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barack_Obama "Barack Obama") struck a warm tone at the [2009 G20 summit in London](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2009_G-20_London_Summit "2009 G-20 London Summit") and released a joint statement that promised a "fresh start" in Russia–United States relations. The statement also called on [Iran](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran "Iran") to abandon its nuclear program and to permit foreign inspectors into the country.[^84]

In March 2009, U.S. secretary of state [Hillary Clinton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hillary_Clinton "Hillary Clinton") and her Russian counterpart [Sergey Lavrov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sergey_Lavrov "Sergey Lavrov") symbolically pressed a "reset" button. The gag fell short as the Russian translation on the button was misspelt by the State Department and actually meant "overload" instead of "reset". After making a few jokes and laughs, they decided to press the button anyway to symbolise friendship.[^85]

In early July 2009, Obama visited Moscow where he had meetings with President Medvedev and Prime Minister Putin. Speaking at the [New Economic School](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Economic_School "New Economic School") Obama told a large gathering, "America wants a strong, peaceful and prosperous Russia. This belief is rooted in our respect for the Russian people, and a shared history between our nations that goes beyond competition." [^86] Days after president Obama's visit to Moscow, U.S. vice president [Joe Biden](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joe_Biden "Joe Biden"), noting that the U.S. was "vastly underestimat\[ing\] the hand that \[it\] h\[e\]ld", told a U.S. newspaper that Russia, with its population base shrinking and the economy "withering", would have to make accommodations to the West on a wide range of national-security issues.[^87]

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Lavrov_and_Clinton_in_NYC-1.jpg/250px-Lavrov_and_Clinton_in_NYC-1.jpg)

Russian foreign minister Sergey Lavrov with U.S. Secretary of State Hillary Clinton at the Waldorf Astoria New York in September 2010

In March 2010, the United States and Russia reached an agreement to reduce their stockpiles of [nuclear weapons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear_weapon "Nuclear weapon"). The new nuclear arms reduction treaty (called [New START](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_START "New START")) was signed by President Obama and President Medvedev on April 8, 2010. The agreement cut the number of long-range nuclear weapons held by each side to about 1,500, down from the current 1,700 to 2,200 set by the [Moscow Treaty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SORT "SORT") of 2002. The New START replaced the [1991 Strategic Arms Reduction Treaty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/START_I "START I"), which expired in December 2009.[^88]

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Vice_President_Joe_Biden_greets_Russian_Prime_Minister_Vladimir_Putin.jpg/250px-Vice_President_Joe_Biden_greets_Russian_Prime_Minister_Vladimir_Putin.jpg)

U.S. vice president Joe Biden and Russian prime minister Vladimir Putin in Moscow, Russia, in March 2011

On a visit to Moscow in March 2011, U.S. vice president Joe Biden reiterated Washington's support for Russia's accession to the [World Trade Organization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_Trade_Organization "World Trade Organization");[^89] he also had a meeting with Russia's leading [human rights](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_rights "Human rights") and opposition leaders where he reportedly told the gathering at the U.S. ambassador's [Spaso House](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spaso_House "Spaso House") residence that it would be better for Russia if Putin did not run for re-election in 2012.[^90]

Through 2020, this was the only time Biden and Putin had met. After an official group meeting Biden characterized in his memoir as "argumentative," he and Putin met privately, with Biden saying "Mr. Prime Minister, I'm looking into your eyes," (a reference to a 2001 meeting between Putin and President Bush, who later said "I looked the man in the eye...I was able to get a sense of his soul"). Biden continued, "I don't think you have a soul." Putin replied, "We understand each other." Biden was elected president in 2020.[^91]

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Biden%2C_berlusconi_and_medvedev.jpeg/250px-Biden%2C_berlusconi_and_medvedev.jpeg)

Joe Biden, Italian prime minister Silvio Berlusconi, and Russian president Dmitry Medvedev meeting in Italy in June 2011

The [2011 military intervention in Libya](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2011_military_intervention_in_Libya "2011 military intervention in Libya") prompted a widespread wave of criticism from several world leaders, including Russian president Medvedev [^92] and Russian prime minister Putin, who said that "\[[UNSC Resolution 1973](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_Resolution_1973 "United Nations Security Council Resolution 1973")\] is defective and flawed...It allows everything. It resembles medieval calls for crusades." [^93]

At the start of the [mass protests](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2011%E2%80%932013_Russian_protests "2011–2013 Russian protests") that began in Russia after the [legislative election](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2011_Russian_legislative_election "2011 Russian legislative election") in early December 2011, prime minister Vladimir Putin accused the United States of interference and inciting unrest, specifically saying that secretary of state Hillary Clinton had sent "a signal" to "some actors in our country"; his comments were seen as indication of a breakdown in the Obama administration's effort to "reset" the relationship.[^94]

By 2012, it was clear that a genuine reset never happened and relations remained sour. Factors in the West included traditional mistrust and fear, an increasing drift away from democracy by Russia, and a demand in Eastern Europe for closer political, economic and military integration with the West. From Russia factors included a move away from democracy by Putin, expectations of regaining superpower status and the tactic of manipulating trade policies and encouraging divisions within NATO.[^95] [^96]

#### Increasing tensions during Putin's third term (2012–2015)

In mid-September 2013, the United States and Russia made a [deal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Destruction_of_Syria%27s_chemical_weapons "Destruction of Syria's chemical weapons") whereby Syria's [chemical weapons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemical_weapons "Chemical weapons") would be placed under international control and eventually destroyed; President Obama welcomed the agreement [^97] that was shortly after enshrined in the [UNSC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council "United Nations Security Council") [Resolution 2118](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_Resolution_2118 "United Nations Security Council Resolution 2118"). The Obama administration was criticised for having used the chemical weapons deal as an ineffectual substitute for military action that Obama had promised in the event of [use of chemical weapons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/U.S._Government_Assessment_of_the_Syrian_Government%27s_Use_of_Chemical_Weapons_on_August_21,_2013 "U.S. Government Assessment of the Syrian Government's Use of Chemical Weapons on August 21, 2013") by the Syrian government.[^98] In [George Robertson](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_Robertson,_Baron_Robertson_of_Port_Ellen "George Robertson, Baron Robertson of Port Ellen") 's view, as well as many others', the failure of Obama to follow through on his 2013 "red line" and take promised military action badly hurt his credibility and that of the United States with Putin and other world leaders.[^99] [^100]

Obama acknowledged Russia's role in securing the [deal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joint_Comprehensive_Plan_of_Action "Joint Comprehensive Plan of Action") to limit [Iran](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran "Iran") 's [nuclear program](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear_program_of_Iran "Nuclear program of Iran") that was reached in July 2015, and personally thanked Putin for Russia's role in the [relevant negotiations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Negotiations_leading_to_the_Joint_Comprehensive_Plan_of_Action "Negotiations leading to the Joint Comprehensive Plan of Action").[^101]

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/US_and_Russian_bases_and_facilities.jpg/250px-US_and_Russian_bases_and_facilities.jpg)

American (red) and Russian (blue) military bases as of 2014

In May 2012, Russian general [Nikolay Yegorovich Makarov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nikolay_Yegorovich_Makarov "Nikolay Yegorovich Makarov") said that there was a possibility of a preemptive strike on [missile defense](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NATO_missile_defence_system "NATO missile defence system") sites in [Eastern Europe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eastern_Europe "Eastern Europe"), to apply pressure to the United States regarding Russia's demands.[^102] Later in August 2012, it was revealed that an [*Akula* -class](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Akula-class_submarine "Akula-class submarine") submarine had conducted a patrol within the [Gulf of Mexico](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico "Gulf of Mexico") without being detected, raising alarms of the U.S. Navy's [anti-submarine warfare](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anti-submarine_warfare "Anti-submarine warfare") capabilities.[^103]

On December 14, 2012, U.S. president Barack Obama signed the [Magnitsky Act](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnitsky_Act "Magnitsky Act"), which "\[imposed\] U.S. travel and financial restrictions on human rights abusers in Russia". On December 28, 2012, Russian president Vladimir Putin signed a [bill](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dima_Yakovlev_Law "Dima Yakovlev Law"), widely seen as retaliatory, that banned any United States citizen from adopting children from Russia.[^104]

On February 12, 2013, hours before the [2013 State of the Union Address](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2013_State_of_the_Union_Address "2013 State of the Union Address") by U.S. president Obama, two Russian [Tu-95 Bear strategic bombers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tupolev_Tu-95 "Tupolev Tu-95"), reportedly equipped with nuclear-tipped cruise missiles, circled the [U.S. territory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unincorporated_territories_of_the_United_States "Unincorporated territories of the United States") of [Guam](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Guam "Guam").[^105] Air Force [F-15 jets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/McDonnell_Douglas_F-15_Eagle "McDonnell Douglas F-15 Eagle") based on [Andersen Air Force Base](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andersen_Air_Force_Base "Andersen Air Force Base") were scrambled to intercept the aircraft.[^105] The Russian aircraft reportedly "were intercepted and left the area in a northbound direction." [^105]

At the end of 2013, Russia announced that a rearmament of the [Kozelsk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/28th_Guards_Rocket_Division "28th Guards Rocket Division"), Novosibirsk, [Tagil Rocket divisions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/42nd_Rocket_Division "42nd Rocket Division") with advanced [RS-24 Yars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RS-24_Yars "RS-24 Yars") intercontinental ballistic missiles was going ahead.[^106]

In July 2014, the U.S. government formally accused Russia of having violated the 1987 [Intermediate-Range Nuclear Forces (INF) Treaty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intermediate-Range_Nuclear_Forces_Treaty "Intermediate-Range Nuclear Forces Treaty") by testing a prohibited medium-range ground-launched [cruise missile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cruise_missile "Cruise missile") (presumably R-500,[^107] a modification of [Iskander](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/9K720_Iskander "9K720 Iskander")) [^108] and threatened to retaliate accordingly.[^108] [^109] Concern in the U.S. was also caused by the test-firing in 2014 of the Russian [RS-26 Rubezh](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RS-26_Rubezh "RS-26 Rubezh") [Intercontinental Ballistic Missile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intercontinental_Ballistic_Missile "Intercontinental Ballistic Missile") capable of evading the existing [anti-ballistic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anti-ballistic_missile "Anti-ballistic missile") [missile defenses](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Missile_defense "Missile defense").[^110] [^111]

In early June 2015, the [U.S. State Department](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Department_of_State "United States Department of State") reported that Russia had failed to correct the violation of the I.N.F. Treaty; the U.S. government was said to have made no discernible headway in making Russia so much as acknowledge the compliance problem.[^112]

#### Edward Snowden affair (2013)

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Edward_Snowden_2013-10-9_%282%29.jpg/250px-Edward_Snowden_2013-10-9_%282%29.jpg)

Snowden in Moscow in October 2013.

[Edward Snowden](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edward_Snowden "Edward Snowden"), a contractor for the United States government, copied and released hundreds of thousands of pages of secret U.S. government documents. He fled to Hong Kong, and then to Russia where in July 2013 he was granted [political asylum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edward_Snowden#Asylum_in_Russia "Edward Snowden"). He was wanted on a criminal warrant by U.S. prosecutors for theft of government property and espionage.[^113]

The granting of asylum further aggravated relations between the two countries and led to the cancellation of a meeting between Obama and Putin that was scheduled for early September 2013 in Moscow.[^114] Snowden remains in Russia as of October 2023.

#### Russian annexation of Crimea (2014)

Following the [collapse](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2014_Ukrainian_revolution "2014 Ukrainian revolution") of the [Viktor Yanukovych](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Viktor_Yanukovych "Viktor Yanukovych") government in [Ukraine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ukraine "Ukraine") in February 2014, Russia annexed [Crimea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crimea "Crimea") on the basis of a controversial [referendum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2014_Crimean_status_referendum "2014 Crimean status referendum") held on March 16, 2014. The U.S. had submitted a UN Security Council resolution declaring the referendum illegal; it was vetoed by Russia on March 15 with China abstaining and the other 13 Security Council members voting for the resolution.[^115] In 2016, in a court in Moscow, former top Ukrainian officials of the Yanukovich administration testified that the collapse of the government was, in their opinion, a [coup d'état](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coup_d%27%C3%A9tat "Coup d'état") organized and sponsored by the U.S. government.[^116] [^117] Russian newspaper [Kommersant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kommersant "Kommersant") alleges [George Friedman](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_Friedman "George Friedman") (chairman of [Stratfor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stratfor "Stratfor")) had agreed this was the "most blatant coup in history', which George Friedman says was taken out of context.[^118] [^119]

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/2014-05-09._%D0%94%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%8C_%D0%9F%D0%BE%D0%B1%D0%B5%D0%B4%D1%8B_%D0%B2_%D0%94%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5%D1%86%D0%BA%D0%B5_237.jpg/250px-2014-05-09._%D0%94%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%8C_%D0%9F%D0%BE%D0%B1%D0%B5%D0%B4%D1%8B_%D0%B2_%D0%94%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5%D1%86%D0%BA%D0%B5_237.jpg)

Anti-American slogans during the Victory Day celebrations in Donetsk, Russian-occupied Ukraine, May 9, 2014.

U.S. secretary of state John Kerry in early March 2014 answering the press questions about Russia's moves in Crimea said, "This is an act of aggression that is completely trumped up in terms of its pretext. It's really 19th century behavior in the 21st century, and there is no way, to start with, that if Russia persists in this, that the G8 countries are going to assemble in Sochi." [^100] On March 24, 2014, the U.S. and its allies in the [G8](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/G8 "G8") political forum suspended Russia's membership thereof.[^120] The decision was dismissed by Russia as inconsequential.[^121] [^122]

At the end of March 2014, U.S. president Obama ruled out any Western military intervention in Ukraine [^121] and admitted that Russia's annexation of Crimea would be hard to reverse; however, he dismissed Russia as a "regional power" that did not pose a major security threat to the U.S.[^123] In January 2016, when asked for his opinion of Obama's statement, Putin said, "I think that speculations about other countries, an attempt to speak disrespectfully about other countries is an attempt to prove one's [exceptionalism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_exceptionalism "American exceptionalism") by contrast. In my view, that is a misguided position." [^124] [^125]

As unrest spread into eastern Ukraine in the spring of 2014, relations between the U.S. and Russia further worsened. The U.S. government imposed punitive [sanctions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_sanctions_during_the_2014_pro-Russian_unrest_in_Ukraine "International sanctions during the 2014 pro-Russian unrest in Ukraine") for Russia's activity in Ukraine. After one bout of sanctions announced by President Obama in July 2014 targeting Russia's major energy, financial and defence companies, Russia said the sanctions would seriously harm the bilateral ties relegating them to the 1980s [Cold War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War "Cold War") era.[^126]

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Vladimir_Putin_and_John_Kerry_%282015-12-15%29_02.jpg/250px-Vladimir_Putin_and_John_Kerry_%282015-12-15%29_02.jpg)

Putin meets with Secretary of State John Kerry, Victoria Nuland and John F. Tefft to discuss Ukraine and other issues in December 2015.

From March 2014 to 2016, six rounds of sanctions were imposed by the U.S., as well as by the EU, and some other countries allied to the U.S. The first three rounds targeted individuals close to Putin by freezing their assets and denying leave to enter. Russia responded by banning import of certain food products as well as by banning entry for certain government officials from the countries that imposed sanctions against Russia.

The end of 2014 saw the passage by the U.S. of the [Ukraine Freedom Support Act of 2014](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ukraine_Freedom_Support_Act_of_2014 "Ukraine Freedom Support Act of 2014"),[^127] [^128] aimed at depriving certain Russian state firms of Western financing and technology while also providing $350 million in arms and military equipment to Ukraine, and the imposition by the U.S. president's [executive order](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Executive_order "Executive order") of yet another round of sanctions.[^129]

Due to the situation concerning Ukraine, relations between Russia and the U.S. that denounced Russia's actions were in 2014 said to be at their worst since the end of the [Cold War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War "Cold War").[^130]

As vice president, Joe Biden urged the Ukrainian government to reduce the nation's reliance on imports of Russian natural gas, and to eliminate pro-Russia middlemen such as [Dmitry Firtash](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dmitry_Firtash "Dmitry Firtash") from the country's natural gas industry.[^131]

#### Russian military intervention in the Syrian Civil War (from September 30, 2015)

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Vladimir_Putin_and_Barack_Obama_%282015-09-29%29_01.jpg/250px-Vladimir_Putin_and_Barack_Obama_%282015-09-29%29_01.jpg)

U.S. president Barack Obama and Russian president Vladimir Putin in New York City, September 2015

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Vladimir_Putin_and_Barack_Obama_%282015-09-29%29_04.jpg/250px-Vladimir_Putin_and_Barack_Obama_%282015-09-29%29_04.jpg)

Barack Obama meets with Vladimir Putin to discuss Syria, September 29, 2015.

Shortly after the start of the [Syrian Civil War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Syrian_Civil_War "Syrian Civil War") in the spring of 2011, the U.S. imposed sanctions on Syria's government and urged President [Bashar al-Assad](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bashar_al-Assad "Bashar al-Assad") to resign; meanwhile, Russia, a long-standing ally of Syria, continued and increased its support for the Syrian government against rebels backed up by the U.S. and its regional allies.

On September 30, 2015, Russia began the [air campaign in Syria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_military_intervention_in_the_Syrian_Civil_War "Russian military intervention in the Syrian Civil War") on the side of the Syrian government headed by President [Bashar al-Assad](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bashar_al-Assad "Bashar al-Assad") of [Syria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Syria "Syria"). According to Russian foreign minister [Sergey Lavrov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sergey_Lavrov "Sergey Lavrov") 's statement made in mid-October 2015, Russia had invited the U.S. to join the Baghdad-based [information center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russia%E2%80%93Syria%E2%80%93Iran%E2%80%93Iraq_coalition "Russia–Syria–Iran–Iraq coalition") set up by Iran, Iraq, Syria and Russia to coordinate their military efforts, but received what he called an "unconstructive" response; Putin's proposal that the U.S. receive a high-level Russian delegation and that a U.S. delegation arrive in Moscow to discuss co-operation in Syria was likewise declined by the U.S.[^132] [^133]

In early October 2015, U.S. president Obama called the way Russia was conducting its military campaign in Syria a "recipe for disaster";[^134] top U.S. military officials ruled out military cooperation with Russia in Syria.[^135] [^136] Secretary of Defense [Ashton Carter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ashton_Carter "Ashton Carter") and other senior U.S. officials said Russia's campaign was primarily aimed at propping up Assad, whom U.S. president Barack Obama had repeatedly called upon to leave power.[^137]

Three weeks into the Russian campaign in Syria, on October 20, 2015, Russian president Vladimir Putin met Bashar al-Assad in Moscow to discuss their joint military campaign and a future political settlement in Syria, according to the Kremlin report of the event.[^138] [^139] The meeting provoked a sharp condemnation from the White House.[^140]

While one of the original aims of the Russian leadership may have been to normalize relations with the U.S. and the West at large, the resultant situation in Syria was said in October 2015 to be a [proxy war](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proxy_war "Proxy war") between Russia and the U.S.[^141] [^142] [^143] [^144] [^145] The two rounds of the [Syria peace talks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Syria_peace_talks_in_Vienna "Syria peace talks in Vienna") held in Vienna in October and November 2015, with Iran participating for the first time, highlighted yet again the deep disagreement over the Syrian settlement between the U.S. and Russia, primarily on the issue of [Bashar al-Assad](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bashar_al-Assad "Bashar al-Assad") 's political future.[^146] The talks in Vienna were followed by a bilateral meeting of Obama and Putin on the sidelines of the [G-20 Summit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2015_G-20_Antalya_summit "2015 G-20 Antalya summit") in Turkey, during which a certain consensus between the two leaders on Syria was reported to have been reached.[^147]

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Secretary_Kerry_Looks_on_as_Russian_Foreign_Minister_Lavrov_Signs_a_Condolence_Book_in_Moscow_%2827710004614%29.jpg/250px-Secretary_Kerry_Looks_on_as_Russian_Foreign_Minister_Lavrov_Signs_a_Condolence_Book_in_Moscow_%2827710004614%29.jpg)

John Kerry and Sergey Lavrov are paying tribute at the French Embassy in Moscow after terror attack in Nice, July 15, 2016.

Bilateral negotiations over Syria were unilaterally suspended by the U.S. on October 3, 2016, which was presented as the U.S. government's reaction to a re-newed [offensive on Aleppo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aleppo_offensive_\(September_2016\) "Aleppo offensive (September 2016)") by Syrian and Russian troops.[^148] On the same day Putin signed a decree [^149] that suspended the 2000 [Plutonium Management and Disposition Agreement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plutonium_Management_and_Disposition_Agreement "Plutonium Management and Disposition Agreement") with the U.S. (the relevant law was signed on October 31, 2016 [^150]), citing the failure by the U.S. to comply with the provisions thereof as well as the U.S.' unfriendly actions that posed a "threat to [strategic stability](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Strategic_stability "Strategic stability")." [^151] [^152]

In mid-October 2016, Russia's U.N. ambassador [Vitaly Churkin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vitaly_Churkin "Vitaly Churkin"), referring to the international situation during the [1973 Arab–Israeli War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yom_Kippur_War "Yom Kippur War"), said that tensions with the U.S. are "probably the worst since 1973".[^153] After two rounds of fruitless talks on Syria in [Lausanne](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lausanne "Lausanne") and London, the foreign ministers of the U.S. and the UK said that additional sanctions against both Russia and Syria were imminent unless Russia and the "Assad regime" stopped their air campaign in Aleppo.[^154] [^155]

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/William_Shepherd_Awarded_Russian_Medal_for_Merit_in_Space_Exploration.jpg/250px-William_Shepherd_Awarded_Russian_Medal_for_Merit_in_Space_Exploration.jpg)

Russian ambassador Sergey Kislyak, NASA Administrator Charles Bolden and William Shepherd after Shepherd was awarded the Russian Medal "For Merit in Space Exploration", December 2, 2016.

### First Trump administration (2017–2021)

#### Election of Donald Trump and Russian interference

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Tax_March_SF_%2834074705055%29.jpg/250px-Tax_March_SF_%2834074705055%29.jpg)

Anti-Trump poster in San Francisco, presumably associating Trump with Russia or the former Soviet Union, April 15, 2017.

In mid-November 2016, shortly after the election of Trump as the U.S. president, the Kremlin accused president Barack Obama's administration of trying to damage the U.S.' relationship with Russia to a degree that would render normalization thereof impossible for Trump's incoming administration.[^156]

In his [address to the Russian parliament](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Presidential_Address_to_the_Federal_Assembly_\(Russia\) "Presidential Address to the Federal Assembly (Russia)") delivered on December 1, 2016, Russian president Putin said this of U.S.—Russia relations: "We are prepared to cooperate with the new American administration. It's important to normalize and begin to develop bilateral relations on an equal and mutually beneficial basis. Mutual efforts by Russia and the United States in solving global and regional problems are in the interest of the entire world." [^157]

In early December 2016, the White House said that President Obama had ordered the intelligence agencies to review evidence of Russian interference in the 2016 presidential campaign; Eric Schultz, the deputy White House press secretary, denied the review to be led by Director of National Intelligence [James R. Clapper](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/James_R._Clapper "James R. Clapper") was meant to be "an effort to challenge the outcome of the election".[^158] Simultaneously, the U.S. press published reports, with reference to senior administration officials, that U.S. intelligence agencies, specifically the [CIA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CIA "CIA"),[^159] had concluded with "high confidence" that Russia acted covertly in the latter stages of the presidential campaign to harm Hillary Clinton's chances and promote Donald Trump.[^160] President-elect Donald Trump rejected the CIA assessment that Russia was behind the hackers' efforts to sway the campaign in his favour as "ridiculous".[^161] [^162]

In mid-December 2016, Hillary Clinton suggested that Putin had a personal grudge against her due to her criticism of the [2011 Russian legislative election](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2011_Russian_legislative_election "2011 Russian legislative election") and his opinion that she was responsible for fomenting the [anti-Putin protests](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2011%E2%80%9313_Russian_protests "2011–13 Russian protests") in Russia that began in December 2011.[^163] She partially attributed her loss in the 2016 election to Russian meddling organized by Putin.[^164] [^165] However, the founder of [WikiLeaks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/WikiLeaks "WikiLeaks"), [Julian Assange](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Julian_Assange "Julian Assange"), has repeatedly denied that Russia was involved in the hack of the emails from the Clinton campaign.[^166] [^167]

Also in mid-December, President Obama publicly pledged to retaliate for Russian cyberattacks during the U.S. presidential election in order to "send a clear message to Russia" as both a punishment and a deterrent;[^168] however, the press reported that his actionable options were limited, with many of those having been rejected as either ineffective or too risky. *[The New York Times](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_New_York_Times "The New York Times")*, citing a catalogue of U.S.-engineered coups in foreign countries, opined, "There is not much new in tampering with elections, except for the technical sophistication of the tools. For all the outrage voiced by Democrats and Republicans in the past week about the Russian action — with the notable exception of Mr. Trump, who has dismissed the intelligence findings as politically motivated — it is worth remembering that trying to manipulate elections is a well-honed American art form." [^169]

The [National Defense Authorization Act for Fiscal Year 2017](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Defense_Authorization_Act_for_Fiscal_Year_2017 "National Defense Authorization Act for Fiscal Year 2017") signed into law by President Obama on December 23, 2016, was criticised by the Russian foreign ministry as yet another attempt to "create problems for the incoming Trump administration and complicate its relations on the international stage, as well as to force it to adopt an anti-Russia policy." [^170]

At the end of 2016, U.S. president-elect Donald Trump praised Putin for not expelling U.S. diplomats in response to Washington's expulsion of 35 Russian diplomats as well as other punitive measures taken by the Obama administration in retaliation for what U.S. officials had characterized as interference in the U.S. presidential election.[^171] [^172]

On January 6, 2017, the [Office of the Director of National Intelligence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Office_of_the_Director_of_National_Intelligence "Office of the Director of National Intelligence") (ODNI), in an assessment of "Russian Activities and Intentions in Recent U.S. Elections", asserted that Russian leadership favored presidential candidate Trump over Clinton, and that Putin personally ordered an "influence campaign" to harm Clinton's chances and "undermine public faith in the US democratic process".[^173] Trump campaign manager Paul Manafort admitted he was in contact with Russian operatives and sharing information through the campaign.[^174]

#### 2017

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Secretary_Tillerson_Listens_as_AP%27s_Josh_Lederman_Asks_a_Question_at_a_Joint_Press_Conference_With_Russian_Foreign_Minister_Lavrov_%2833156239434%29.jpg/330px-Secretary_Tillerson_Listens_as_AP%27s_Josh_Lederman_Asks_a_Question_at_a_Joint_Press_Conference_With_Russian_Foreign_Minister_Lavrov_%2833156239434%29.jpg)

Secretary of State Rex Tillerson with Russian foreign minister Sergey Lavrov in Moscow, Russia, April 12, 2017.

A week after [Trump's inauguration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/First_inauguration_of_Donald_Trump "First inauguration of Donald Trump") on January 20, 2017, [Trump](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Donald_Trump "Donald Trump") had a 52-minute telephone conversation with Russian president [Vladimir Putin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Putin "Vladimir Putin") that was hailed by both governments as a step towards improvement of relations between the U.S. and Russia; the presidents agreed to arrange a face-to-face meeting for a later date.[^175] [^176]

In early March 2017, the U.S. military for the first time publicly accused Russia of having deployed a land-based cruise missile ([SSC-8](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RK-55 "RK-55") [^177]) that they said violated the "spirit and intent" of the 1987 Intermediate-range Nuclear Forces (INF) treaty and posed a threat to NATO.[^178]

On March 25, 2017, the U.S. imposed new sanctions against eight Russian companies in connection with the [Iran, North Korea, Syria Nonproliferation Act](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran,_North_Korea,_Syria_Nonproliferation_Act "Iran, North Korea, Syria Nonproliferation Act") (INKSNA).
