The [cruise-missile strikes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2017_Shayrat_missile_strike "2017 Shayrat missile strike") on the Syrian [Shayrat Airbase](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shayrat_Airbase "Shayrat Airbase"), conducted by the U.S. on April 7, 2017, as a response to the [Khan Shaykhun chemical attack](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Khan_Shaykhun_chemical_attack "Khan Shaykhun chemical attack"),[^179] [^180] [^181] were condemned by Russia as an "act of aggression" that was based on a "trumped-up pretext", which substantially impaired Russia–United States relations.[^182] Russian prime minister [Dmitry Medvedev](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dmitry_Medvedev "Dmitry Medvedev") said the attack had placed the U.S. on the cusp of warfare with Russia.[^183] [^184] [^185] Both Donald Trump in April and the Russian government in May characterised the relationship between the countries as frozen and lacking any progress;[^186] [^187] in early June, Vladimir Putin said relations were at an all-time low since the end of the [Cold War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War "Cold War").[^188] In mid-June 2017, the Russian foreign ministry confirmed that, for the first time ever, Russia had failed to receive a formal greeting from the U.S. government on occasion of [Russia's national day](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russia_Day "Russia Day") celebrated on June 12.[^189] [^190] [^191] [^192]

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Secretary_Tillerson_and_Russian_Foreign_Minister_Lavrov_Address_Reporters_Before_Their_Meeting_in_Washington_%2834411963832%29.jpg/330px-Secretary_Tillerson_and_Russian_Foreign_Minister_Lavrov_Address_Reporters_Before_Their_Meeting_in_Washington_%2834411963832%29.jpg)

U.S. secretary of state Rex Tillerson and Russian foreign minister Sergey Lavrov in Washington, D.C., May 10, 2017.

In April 2017, Trump's administration denied a request from [ExxonMobil](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ExxonMobil "ExxonMobil") to allow it to resume [oil drilling in Russia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Petroleum_industry_in_Russia "Petroleum industry in Russia").[^193] In July 2017, ExxonMobil filed a lawsuit against the U.S. government challenging the finding that the company violated sanctions imposed on Russia.[^194]

On May 10, 2017, Trump had an unannounced meeting in the [Oval Office](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oval_Office "Oval Office") with Russian foreign minister [Sergey Lavrov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sergey_V._Lavrov "Sergey V. Lavrov") and Russian ambassador to the United States [Sergey Kislyak](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sergey_I._Kislyak "Sergey I. Kislyak").[^195] During the meeting he disclosed highly classified information, providing details that could have been used to deduce the source of the information and the manner in which it was collected, according to current and former government officials.[^196] [^197] Although the disclosure was not illegal, it was widely criticized because of the possible danger to the source.[^198] [^199]

On July 6, 2017, during a speech in [Warsaw](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Warsaw "Warsaw"), Poland, Trump urged Russia to cease its support for "hostile regimes" in [Syria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Syria "Syria") and [Iran](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran "Iran").[^200] On July 7, 2017, in what appeared to be a sign of good relations between the leaders of both countries,[^201] Trump met with Putin at the [G20 Hamburg summit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2017_G20_Hamburg_summit "2017 G20 Hamburg summit") in Germany and described the meeting as "an honour." [^202]

In mid-July 2017, the Russian foreign ministry noted that the staff of the [U.S. Embassy in Moscow](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Embassy_of_the_United_States,_Moscow "Embassy of the United States, Moscow"), following expulsion of diplomats by the Obama administration in December 2016, far exceeded the number of Russian embassy employees in Washington and indicated that the Russian government was considering retaliatory expulsion of more than thirty-five U.S. diplomats, thus evening out the number of the countries' diplomats posted.[^203] On July 28, Russia announced punitive measures that were cast as Russia's response to the [additional, codified, sanctions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Countering_America%27s_Adversaries_Through_Sanctions_Act "Countering America's Adversaries Through Sanctions Act") against Moscow passed by Congress days prior, but also referenced the specific measures imposed against the Russian diplomatic mission in the U.S. by the Obama administration.[^204] Russia demanded that the U.S. reduce its diplomatic and technical personnel in the Moscow embassy and its consulates in St Petersburg, Ekaterinburg and Vladivostok to four hundred fifty-five persons — the same as the number of Russian diplomats posted in the U.S. — by September 1; Russia's government would also suspend the use of a retreat compound and a storage facility in Moscow used by the U.S. by August 1.[^204] [^205] [^206] Two days later, Vladimir Putin said that the decision on the curtailment of the U.S. diplomatic mission personnel had been taken by him personally and that 755 staff must terminate their work in Russia.[^207] [^208] [^209] After the sanction bill was on August 2 signed by Donald Trump, Russian prime minister [Dmitry Medvedev](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dmitry_Medvedev "Dmitry Medvedev") wrote that the law had ended hope for improving U.S.–Russia relations and meant "an all-out trade war with Russia." [^210] [^211] The law was also criticised by Donald Trump, whose [signing statement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Signing_statement "Signing statement") indicated that he might choose not to enforce certain provisions of the legislation that he deemed unconstitutional.[^212] [^213]

Russia protested on September 2, 2017, against a search it said U.S. officials were planning of a Russian trade mission building in Washington D.C., shortly after the U.S., "in the spirit of parity invoked by the Russians", demanded that Russia shut two of its diplomatic annexes (buildings) in Washington D.C. and New York City as well as its [Consulate General](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Consulate-General_of_Russia_in_San_Francisco "Consulate-General of Russia in San Francisco") in [San Francisco](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/San_Francisco "San Francisco").[^214] The Russian foreign ministry said the inspection would be "illegal" and an "unprecedented aggressive action"; it also demanded that the U.S. ″immediately return the Russian diplomatic facilities″.[^215] [^216]

In November 2017, Trump and Putin both attended the [Asia-Pacific Economic Cooperation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asia-Pacific_Economic_Cooperation "Asia-Pacific Economic Cooperation") meeting in [Danang](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Danang "Danang"). Although they had no formal meeting they spoke informally several times during the event.[^217]

At the end of 2017, CNN concluded that a series of steps undertaken by the first Trump administration within a mere week before [Christmas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christmas "Christmas") such as naming Russia a "rival power" and ″revisionist power″ (along with [China](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/China "China")), imposing sanctions on [Ramzan Kadyrov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ramzan_Kadyrov "Ramzan Kadyrov"), a close Putin ally, the decision to provide Ukraine with anti-tank weapons, coupled with tougher line from the State Department about Moscow's activities in eastern Ukraine, and accusations from the Pentagon that Russia was intentionally violating de-confliction agreements in Syria, highlighted "a decided turn away from the warmer, more cooperative relationship with Russia that President Donald Trump called for during his campaign and early in his presidency".[^218] [^219] [^220] In February 2018, echoing Donald Trump's own statement, White House press secretary [Sarah Huckabee Sanders](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sarah_Huckabee_Sanders "Sarah Huckabee Sanders") said: "\[President Donald Trump\] has been tougher on Russia in the first year than Obama was in eight years combined." [^221] [^222]

#### Beginning of Putin's fourth term (2018–2020)

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/World_nuclear_weapons.png/250px-World_nuclear_weapons.png)

Large nuclear weapons stockpile with global range (dark blue), smaller stockpile with global range (medium blue).

A highly unusual [^223] unannounced visit to Washington, D.C., at the end of January 2018 by the directors of Russia's [three main intelligence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intelligence_agencies_of_Russia "Intelligence agencies of Russia") and security agencies ([FSB](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Federal_Security_Service "Federal Security Service"), [SVR](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Foreign_Intelligence_Service_\(Russia\) "Foreign Intelligence Service (Russia)"), and [GRU](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/GRU_\(Russian_Federation\) "GRU (Russian Federation)")), two of whom ([Sergey Naryshkin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sergey_Naryshkin "Sergey Naryshkin") and [Igor Korobov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Igor_Korobov "Igor Korobov")) were on the U.S. sanctions list,[^224] and their reported meetings with top U.S. security officials caused political controversy in the U.S. and elicited no official comment in Russia, while it occurred days before the first Trump administration chose not to impose immediately new sanctions on Russia at the deadline mandated by the [Countering America's Adversaries Through Sanctions Act](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Countering_America%27s_Adversaries_Through_Sanctions_Act "Countering America's Adversaries Through Sanctions Act").[^225] [^226] [^227] [^228]

The [U.S. air and artillery strike](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Battle_of_Khasham "Battle of Khasham") on a pro-government formation in eastern Syria on February 7, 2018, which caused massive death toll among Russian nationals and a political scandal in Russia, was billed by media as "the first deadly clash between citizens of Russia and the United States since the [Cold War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War "Cold War") " and "an episode that threatens to deepen tensions with Moscow".[^229] [^230]

Public statements read out by Vladimir Putin on March 1, 2018, days before the [presidential election](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2018_Russian_presidential_election "2018 Russian presidential election"), about missile technology breakthroughs made by Russia, were referred to by first Trump administration officials as largely boastful untruths, as well as confirmation that "Russia ha\[d\] been developing destabilizing weapons systems for over a decade, in direct violation of its treaty obligations".[^231] U.S. Defense Secretary James Mattis remarked that the systems Putin had talked about "\[were\] still years away" and he did not see them changing the military balance.[^232] Nevertheless, White House insiders were later quoted as saying that Putin's claims "really got under the president \[Trump\]'s skin" and caused Trump to take a sharper tone behind the scenes vis-à-vis Vladimir Putin.[^233]

On March 26, 2018, following the [United States National Security Council](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_National_Security_Council "United States National Security Council") 's recommendation,[^234] to demonstrate the U.S.'s support for the UK's position on the [Salisbury poisoning incident](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Poisoning_of_Sergei_and_Yulia_Skripal "Poisoning of Sergei and Yulia Skripal"), President Donald Trump ordered the expulsion of sixty Russian diplomats and closure of Russian consulate in Seattle.[^235] [^236] Russian foreign minister Sergei Lavrov responded to the simultaneous expulsion of the total of 140 Russian diplomats by 25 countries by accusing the U.S. government of "blackmailing" other nations.[^237] [^238]

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Vladimir_Putin_%26_Donald_Trump_in_Helsinki%2C_16_July_2018_%284%29.jpg/330px-Vladimir_Putin_%26_Donald_Trump_in_Helsinki%2C_16_July_2018_%284%29.jpg)

Talks between U.S. delegation headed by Trump and Russian delegation headed by Vladimir Putin at the summit in Helsinki, Finland July 16, 2018.

In April 2018, U.S.–Russian relations were further exacerbated by [missile strikes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2018_missile_strikes_against_Syria "2018 missile strikes against Syria") against the Syrian government targets following the [suspected chemical attack](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Douma_chemical_attack "Douma chemical attack") in Douma on April 7.[^239] The countries clashed diplomatically, with Russia's top military officials threatening to hit U.S. military targets in the event of a massive U.S.-led strike against Syria.[^240] [^241] [^242] [^243] In late May, during an interview with [RT](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russia_Today "Russia Today"), Syria's president [Bashar al-Assad](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bashar_al-Assad "Bashar al-Assad") said that direct military conflict between the Russian forces and the U.S. forces in Syria had been averted in April "by the wisdom of the Russian leadership" and that the U.S.-led missile attack against Syria would have been far more extensive had it not been for Russia's intervention.[^244] [^245]

On June 8, 2018, Trump called for Russia to be readmitted to the [G-7](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Group_of_Seven "Group of Seven"), from which it was expelled after the [Russian annexation of Crimea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Annexation_of_Crimea_by_the_Russian_Federation "Annexation of Crimea by the Russian Federation") in 2014.[^246]

Trump's public statements during his [first formal meeting](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2018_Russia%E2%80%93United_States_Summit "2018 Russia–United States Summit") with Putin in Helsinki on July 16, 2018, drew criticism from the Democratic members of the U.S. Congress and a number of former senior intelligence officials as well as some ranking members of the Republican party for appearing to have sided with Putin rather than accepting the findings of Russian interference in the 2016 presidential election issued by the [United States Intelligence Community](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Intelligence_Community "United States Intelligence Community").[^247] [^248] [^249] Republican senator [John McCain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_McCain "John McCain") called the press conference "one of the most disgraceful performances by an American president in memory." [^250] The press around the world ran publications that tended to assess the news conference following the presidents′ two-hour meeting as an event at which Trump had "projected weakness".[^251]

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/President_Trump_at_the_G20_%2848144140002%29.jpg/330px-President_Trump_at_the_G20_%2848144140002%29.jpg)

Donald Trump (center), U.S. secretary of state Mike Pompeo and Russian president Vladimir Putin (left) meet in Osaka, Japan, in June 2019.

In December 2019, the first Trump administration imposed sanctions on businesses involved in the construction of [Nord Stream 2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nord_Stream_2 "Nord Stream 2") natural gas pipeline from Russia to [Germany](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Germany "Germany"),[^252] as the U.S. sought to sell more of its own [liquefied natural gas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Liquefied_natural_gas "Liquefied natural gas") (LNG) to European states.[^253] German Finance Minister [Olaf Scholz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Olaf_Scholz "Olaf Scholz") called the sanctions "a severe intervention in German and European internal affairs", while the EU spokesman criticized "the imposition of sanctions against EU companies conducting legitimate business." [^254] Russian foreign minister [Sergey Lavrov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sergey_Lavrov "Sergey Lavrov") also criticized sanctions, saying that [U.S. Congress](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Congress "United States Congress") "is literally overwhelmed with the desire to do everything to destroy" the U.S.–Russia relations.[^255]

A June 2020 *[New York Times](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_York_Times "New York Times")* report, citing unnamed sources, stated that American intelligence officials assessed with medium confidence that Russian military intelligence unit [29155](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unit_29155 "Unit 29155") had [supervised a bounty program](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_bounty_program "Russian bounty program") paying [Taliban](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taliban "Taliban") -linked militants to kill foreign servicemembers, including Americans, in Afghanistan in 2019.[^256] [^257] The bounty program reportedly resulted in the deaths of "several" U.S. soldiers.[^258] [The Pentagon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Pentagon "The Pentagon") 's top leaders said that the Russian bounty program could not be corroborated.[^259] The Taliban and Russia have both denied that the bounty program exists.[^258] President [Donald Trump](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Donald_Trump "Donald Trump") and his aides denied that he was briefed on the intelligence. Director of National Intelligence [John Ratcliffe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Ratcliffe_\(American_politician\) "John Ratcliffe (American politician)") said that Trump had not received a briefing on the bounty program. White House press secretary [Kayleigh McEnany](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kayleigh_McEnany "Kayleigh McEnany") said the same.[^258] Secretary of Defense [Mark Esper](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mark_Esper "Mark Esper") said that General [Kenneth McKenzie](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kenneth_F._McKenzie_Jr. "Kenneth F. McKenzie Jr."), the commander of U.S. Central Command, and General [Scott Miller](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Austin_S._Miller "Austin S. Miller"), the top U.S. military commander in Afghanistan, did not think "the reports were credible as they dug into them." [^259] McKenzie said that he found no "causative link" between reported bounties to actual U.S. military deaths, but said a lack of proof is "often true in battlefield intelligence." [^259]

On July 1, 2020, following media reports of Taliban participation in an alleged Russian bounty program, the U.S. [House Armed Services Committee](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_House_Committee_on_Armed_Services "United States House Committee on Armed Services") overwhelmingly voted in favor of an amendment to restrict President Trump's ability to withdraw U.S. troops from [Afghanistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghanistan "Afghanistan").[^260]

On September 25, 2020, [U.S. Air Force](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/U.S._Air_Force "U.S. Air Force") [B-52](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boeing_B-52_Stratofortress "Boeing B-52 Stratofortress") bombers staged a mock attack run on [Kaliningrad](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kaliningrad "Kaliningrad"), a Russian exclave locked between NATO countries. The simulated raid on the [Kaliningrad region](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kaliningrad_Oblast "Kaliningrad Oblast") was a test case of destroying Russian air defense systems located in the region.[^261]

#### Influence on the Trump administrations

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Vladimir_Putin_%26_Donald_Trump_in_Helsinki%2C_16_July_2018_%2810%29.jpg/250px-Vladimir_Putin_%26_Donald_Trump_in_Helsinki%2C_16_July_2018_%2810%29.jpg)

The 2018 Helsinki summit. Putin gifts Trump a Telstar Mechta, the official match ball for the knockout stage of the 2018 FIFA World Cup.

Shortly before the inauguration of President Trump, the [Steele dossier](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Steele_dossier "Steele dossier") was leaked to the public. Written by a [private intelligence firm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christopher_Steele "Christopher Steele") claiming to unearth a relationship between his [presidential campaign](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Donald_Trump_2016_presidential_campaign "Donald Trump 2016 presidential campaign") and the Russian government, the report alleged that the Russians possessed [kompromat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kompromat "Kompromat") on Trump which could be used to [blackmail](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blackmail "Blackmail") him. It suggested the Kremlin had promised the campaign that compromising information would not be released if the Administration cooperated.[^262] [^263] Though the report was met with skepticism, the relationship between Russian leadership and the incoming first Trump Administration became highly salient. Days later, [Ynet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ynet "Ynet"), an Israeli online news site, reported that U.S. intelligence had advised [Israeli intelligence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israeli_Intelligence_Community "Israeli Intelligence Community") officers to be cautious about sharing information with the [incoming first Trump administration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/First_presidential_transition_of_Donald_Trump "First presidential transition of Donald Trump") until the possibility of Russian influence over Trump had been fully investigated.[^264] Allegations of collusion between Trump associations and the Russian government continued to emerge well into his presidency.

Various [links between Trump associates and Russian officials](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Links_between_Trump_associates_and_Russian_officials "Links between Trump associates and Russian officials") have been documented and heavily scrutinized, most notably former National Security Advisor Michael Flynn's [contacts with the Russian ambassador](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_v._Flynn#Contacts_with_the_Russian_ambassador,_public_statements_about_these "United States v. Flynn"). Throughout his presidential tenure, Trump expressed both support and criticism of Russia's actions in Crimea,[^265] Syria,[^266] Ukraine,[^267] North Korea,[^268] Venezuela,[^269] election meddling,[^270] Skripal poisoning,[^271] and oil drilling in Russia.[^272]

In spite of a halting of the FBI's efforts to corroborate the Steele dossier's allegations, some progress has been made, so the [corroboration status of its allegations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Steele_dossier#Corroboration_status_with_passage_of_time "Steele dossier") has changed with time as more information has come forth. Some allegations have been publicly corroborated, while some others are plausible but not specifically confirmed, and yet others are dubious in retrospect but not strictly disproven.[^2] Some have described the allegations as part of a [conspiracy theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conspiracy_theory "Conspiracy theory").[^3]

Trump's actions at the [Helsinki summit in 2018](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2018_Russia%E2%80%93United_States_Summit "2018 Russia–United States Summit") led some to conclude that Steele's report was more accurate than not. [Politico](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Politico "Politico") reported, "Trump sided with the Russians over the U.S. intelligence community's assessment that Moscow had waged an all-out attack on the 2016 election...The joint news conference cemented fears among some that Trump was in Putin's pocket and prompted bipartisan backlash." [^294] At the joint news conference, when asked directly about the subject, Putin denied that he had any kompromat on Trump. Trump was reportedly given a gift from Putin the weekend of the pageant, though Putin argued "that he did not even know Trump was in Russia for the [Miss Universe pageant in 2013](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Miss_Universe_2013 "Miss Universe 2013") when, according to the Steele dossier, video of Trump was secretly recorded to blackmail him." [^295]

In reaction to Trump's actions at the summit, Senator [Chuck Schumer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charles_E._Schumer "Charles E. Schumer") (D-N.Y.) spoke in the Senate:

> Millions of Americans will continue to wonder if the only possible explanation for this dangerous and inexplicable behavior is the possibility — the very real possibility — that President Putin holds damaging information over President Trump.[^296]

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Director_of_National_Intelligence_James_Clapper.jpg/250px-Director_of_National_Intelligence_James_Clapper.jpg)

In May 2017, James Clapper, the former director of national intelligence, told NBC 's Meet the Press that Russians are "almost genetically driven" to act deviously. 297

Several operatives and lawyers in the U.S. intelligence community reacted strongly to Trump's performance at the summit, describing it as "subservien\[ce\] to Putin" and "a fervent defense of Russia's military and cyber aggression around the world, and its violation of international law in Ukraine". Some framed Trump's conduct as harmful to U.S. interests and an asset to Russian interests, suggesting that he was a "useful idiot" to Putin,[^298] and that he looked like "Putin's puppet".[^299] Former [Director of National Intelligence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Director_of_National_Intelligence "Director of National Intelligence") [James Clapper](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/James_Clapper "James Clapper") wondered "if Russians have something on Trump",[^300] and former CIA director [John O. Brennan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_O._Brennan "John O. Brennan") accused Trump of treason, tweeting: "He is wholly in the pocket of Putin." [^301] In January 2019, former acting CIA director [Michael Morell](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Michael_Morell "Michael Morell") called Trump "an unwitting agent of the Russian federation", echoing the sentiments of former CIA director [Michael V. Hayden](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Michael_Hayden_\(general\) "Michael Hayden (general)").[^302] House Speaker [Nancy Pelosi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nancy_Pelosi "Nancy Pelosi") suggested then-president Trump's behavior was part of a pattern: "All roads lead to Putin." [^303]

### Biden administration (2021–2025)

Following the arrest of Russian opposition leader [Alexei Navalny](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexei_Navalny "Alexei Navalny") on January 17, 2021, [Jake Sullivan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jake_Sullivan "Jake Sullivan"), Biden's national security advisor, stated: "Mr. Navalny should be immediately released, and the perpetrators of the outrageous attack on his life must be held accountable. The Kremlin's attacks on Mr. Navalny are not just a violation of human rights, but an affront to the Russian people who want their voices heard." [^304]

On the day of Biden's inauguration, Russia urged the new administration to take a "more constructive" approach in talks over the extension of the [2010 New START treaty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_START "New START"), accusing the first Trump administration of "deliberately and intentionally" dismantling international arms control agreements and attacking its "counterproductive and openly aggressive" approach in talks.[^305] On January 26, Biden and Putin agreed that they would extend by five years the New START treaty, which would otherwise have expired in February 2021.[^306]

On March 17, 2021, the Russian foreign ministry announced that Russia had recalled [its ambassador to the U.S.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_ambassadors_of_Russia_to_the_United_States "List of ambassadors of Russia to the United States"), [Anatoly Antonov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anatoly_Antonov "Anatoly Antonov"), for "consultations" in a move that was characterized by the ministry's spokesperson as being without precedent for a Russia ambassador to the U.S.[^307] The recall came after Biden said he thought that Putin was "a killer" and said he would "pay the price" for the [interference in the 2020 U.S. election](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_interference_in_the_2020_United_States_elections "Russian interference in the 2020 United States elections"), which had been confirmed by a declassified [DNI](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Director_of_National_Intelligence "Director of National Intelligence") [report released the previous day](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_interference_in_the_2020_United_States_elections#The_2021_DNI_report "Russian interference in the 2020 United States elections").[^308] The State Department commented on the recall by saying that while the U.S. would work with Russia to advance U.S. interests, they would "be able to hold Russia accountable for any of their malign actions".[^309]

On April 15, the U.S. announced the expulsion of 10 Russian diplomats and imposed sanctions on six Russian technology companies as well as 32 other individuals and entities. The new sanctions also targeted [ruble-denominated sovereign debt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/OFZ_\(bond\) "OFZ (bond)"). Nevertheless, the economic punishments were assessed by observers as "more bark than bite" and likely to be "largely symbolic", with the ruble even rebounding against the dollar on the news. Biden commented the United States "could have gone further" with the sanctions, but that he had opted for a milder form of sovereign-debt sanctions for now because he wanted to avoid a "cycle of escalation and conflict." [^310] Russia retaliated the following day, expelling 10 U.S. diplomats and suggesting the [U.S. ambassador](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_J._Sullivan_\(diplomat\) "John J. Sullivan (diplomat)") return home for consultations.[^311]

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Putin_in_Switzerland_2021_09.jpg/250px-Putin_in_Switzerland_2021_09.jpg)

US president Joe Biden and Russian president Vladimir Putin at the summit meeting in Geneva, June 2021

On May 19, the [Biden administration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Presidency_of_Joe_Biden "Presidency of Joe Biden") lifted sanctions on the [Nord Stream 2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nord_Stream_2 "Nord Stream 2") gas pipeline that was being built between Russia and [Germany](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Germany "Germany"). While President Biden believed the project was bad, the U.S. State Department explained it had concluded that it was in the "U.S. national interest" to waive the sanctions.[^312]

In May 2021, Biden and Putin agreed to meet as the relationship between the countries was being assessed to be at the lowest point since the 1980s.[^313] At the [meeting in Geneva in mid-June](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2021_Russia%E2%80%93United_States_Summit "2021 Russia–United States Summit"), the countries′ leaders reached an agreement to return their ambassadors to their posts in each other's capitals, no progress was made in overcoming the major points of contention.[^314]

On August 21, the Department of State imposed increased sanctions on Russia for alleged poisoning of Alexei Navalny. These sanctions include a ban on ammunition imports into the United States, as well as restrictions of [small arm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Small_arms_trade "Small arms trade") sales.[^315]

On December 1, 2021, Russia's [Foreign Ministry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ministry_of_Foreign_Affairs_\(Russia\) "Ministry of Foreign Affairs (Russia)") told U.S. diplomats who have been working in Moscow for more than three years, to leave the country by January 31, 2022.[^316] The move came in response to news on November 28, 2021, that the US would be expelling 27 Russian diplomatic staff by the end of January 2022.[^317]

On February 21, 2023, Russian president Vladimir Putin suspended the New START agreement.

#### 2021–2022 Russo-Ukrainian crisis

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Secretary_Blinken_Meets_With_Russian_Foreign_Minister_Lavrov_%2851719522528%29.jpg/250px-Secretary_Blinken_Meets_With_Russian_Foreign_Minister_Lavrov_%2851719522528%29.jpg)

Russian foreign minister Sergey Lavrov meets with U.S. secretary of state Antony Blinken on December 2, 2021

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Talks_between_Vladimir_Putin_and_President_of_the_United_States_Joseph_Biden.jpg/250px-Talks_between_Vladimir_Putin_and_President_of_the_United_States_Joseph_Biden.jpg)

U.S. president Joe Biden holds a video call with Russian president Vladimir Putin, on December 7, 2021

In late 2021 and early 2022, Russian troops build up along the Russo-Ukrainian border, resulted in renewed tensions between Russia and NATO. Senior officials of the [Biden administration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Presidency_of_Joe_Biden "Presidency of Joe Biden") reported that Russia had only withdrawn a few thousand troops since the previous military buildup in early 2021. *The New York Times* estimated over 80,000 Russian troops still remain at the Russo-Ukrainian border by September 2021.[^318] The Kremlin repeatedly denied that it had any plans to invade Ukraine.[^319] [^320]

On November 30, 2021, Putin stated that an [expansion of NATO's presence in Ukraine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Enlargement_of_NATO "Enlargement of NATO"), especially the deployment of any [long-range missiles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ballistic_missile "Ballistic missile") capable of striking Russian cities or [missile defence systems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_national_missile_defense "United States national missile defense") similar to those in Romania and Poland, would be a "red line" issue for the Kremlin.[^321] [^322] [^323] Putin asked President [Joe Biden](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joe_Biden "Joe Biden") for legal guarantees that NATO wouldn't expand eastward or put "weapons systems that threaten us in close vicinity to Russian territory." [^324] The U.S. rejected Putin's demands.[^325] [^326]

Biden and Putin discussed the crisis over the course of a 50-minute phone call on December 30, 2021.[^327] Bilateral talks began in [Geneva](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geneva "Geneva") on January 10, 2022, to discuss the [Russo-Ukrainian war](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russo-Ukrainian_war "Russo-Ukrainian war") as well as longstanding Russian concerns regarding NATO postering in Eastern Europe.[^328] The talks were led by Russian Deputy Foreign Minister [Sergei Ryabkov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sergei_Ryabkov "Sergei Ryabkov") and U.S. Deputy Secretary of State [Wendy Sherman](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wendy_Sherman "Wendy Sherman").[^329] On January 31, 2022, both the United States and Russia discussed the crisis at an emergency meeting of the UN Security Council.[^330] The discussion was tense, with both sides accusing the other of stoking tensions.

The United States government increased military support to Ukraine through a $650 million arms deal.[^331] U.S. Secretary of Defense [Lloyd Austin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lloyd_Austin "Lloyd Austin") and [Chairman](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chairman_of_the_Joint_Chiefs_of_Staff "Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff") of the Joint Chiefs of Staff [Mark Milley](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mark_Milley "Mark Milley") threatened U.S. support for an anti-Russian insurgency within Ukraine.[^332] The Biden administration approved deliveries of American-made [FIM-92 Stinger](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/FIM-92_Stinger "FIM-92 Stinger") [surface-to-air missiles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surface-to-air_missile "Surface-to-air missile") to Ukraine.[^333] The government threatened severe sanctions against Russia as well as personal sanctions against Putin and his allies.[^334] The United States also threatened to halt the opening of the [Nord Stream 2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nord_Stream_2 "Nord Stream 2") pipeline that would send Russian [natural gas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural_gas "Natural gas") to Germany, "if Russia invades Ukraine one way or another." [^335]

In January 2022, the United States accused Russia of sending [saboteurs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sabotage "Sabotage") into Ukraine to stage "a false-flag operation" that would create a pretext for Russia to invade Ukraine. Russian foreign minister [Sergey Lavrov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sergey_Lavrov "Sergey Lavrov") dismissed the U.S. claim as "total disinformation." [^320] On February 4, 2022, Lavrov dismissed as "nonsense" and "craziness" allegations by the United States that Russia was preparing a fake video of the Ukrainian forces attacking the separatist-held [Donbas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Donbas "Donbas") as a pretext for starting a war in Ukraine.[^336]

On January 19, 2022, President Biden said that he believed Russia would invade Ukraine.[^337] Biden said a full-scale invasion of Ukraine would be "the most consequential thing that's happened in the world in terms of war and peace" since [World War II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_War_II "World War II").[^338] Biden and Ukrainian president [Volodymyr Zelenskyy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Volodymyr_Zelenskyy "Volodymyr Zelenskyy") disagreed on how imminent the threat was.[^339] [^340] On February 10, 2022, Biden urged all American citizens in Ukraine to leave immediately.[^341] On February 11, 2022, Biden's national security advisor [Jake Sullivan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jake_Sullivan "Jake Sullivan") publicly warned about the likelihood of a Russian invasion of Ukraine prior to the end of the [2022 Winter Olympics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2022_Winter_Olympics "2022 Winter Olympics").[^342]

The [Chicago Council on Global Affairs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chicago_Council_on_Global_Affairs "Chicago Council on Global Affairs") poll, conducted on July 26, 2021, found that 50% of Americans supported the use of [U.S. troops](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Armed_Forces "United States Armed Forces") to defend Ukraine if Russia invaded the rest of the country.[^343]

In December 2021, a [Levada Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Levada_Center "Levada Center") poll found that about 50% of Russians believed that the U.S. and NATO were responsible for the Russo-Ukrainian crisis, while 16% blamed Ukraine and just 4% blamed Russia.[^344] [^345]

In February 2022, according to the White House, U.S. president Joe Biden stated in a video conference with Russian president Vladimir Putin that if Russia invades Ukraine, Washington and its allies will respond "decisively and impose fast and severe penalties." [^346]

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/President_Biden_at_Camp_David_February_12_2022.jpg/250px-President_Biden_at_Camp_David_February_12_2022.jpg)

A majority of Americans disapprove of President Joe Biden 's handling of the Russo-Ukrainian crisis. 347

On February 16, 2022, the U.S. State Department stated that Russia is seeking to establish a "pretext" for invading Ukraine by making unsubstantiated claims of "genocide" and mass graves in Ukraine's eastern Donbas region.[^348]

On February 20, 2022, the US secretary of state showed his concern about the continuation of Russian military drills in Belarus. According to Antony Blinken, Moscow's decision to keep roughly 30,000 troops in Belarus, near to Ukraine, amid increased tensions in the east justifies US's concerns.[^349]

On February 22, 2022, U.S. president Joe Biden criticized Russia's [recognition of the Donetsk People's Republic and the Luhansk People's Republic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_recognition_of_the_Donetsk_People%27s_Republic_and_the_Luhansk_People%27s_Republic "International recognition of the Donetsk People's Republic and the Luhansk People's Republic") as "the beginning of the Russian invasion of Ukraine" and announced sanctions against on banks VEB and [Promsvyazbank](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Promsvyazbank "Promsvyazbank") and comprehensive sanctions on Russia's sovereign debt in response.[^350]

#### Russian invasion of Ukraine and significantly increased tensions

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/-StopPutinNow_Rally_NYC_%2851903882696%29.jpg/250px--StopPutinNow_Rally_NYC_%2851903882696%29.jpg)

Protest against the war outside the Russian Consulate in New York City on February 24, 2022

On February 24, 2022, Russia launched an invasion of Ukraine opening fire with explosive ordinance and hitting several residential buildings, by the 25th the invading army had taken all of the Chernobyl exclusion zone and began to attack the Ukrainian capital with high resistance from both the Ukrainian military and a makeshift militia. On February 26, President Joe Biden authorized the US State Department to deliver up to $350 million in weapons from US stockpiles to Ukraine.[^351]

President Joe Biden rejected the idea of a NATO-enforced [no-fly zone](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/No-fly_zone "No-fly zone") over Ukraine, in order to avoid a direct war with Russia.[^352]

On February 26, 2022, the deputy head of Russia's Security Council chaired by President Vladimir Putin, warned that Moscow may retaliate to international sanctions by withdrawing from the most recent nuclear arms treaty with the US, severing diplomatic ties with Western nations, and freezing their assets.[^353]

On February 28, 2022, the U.S., during a meeting with the U.N., asked the Russian ambassador, [Vasily Nebenzya](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vasily_Nebenzya "Vasily Nebenzya"), to remove 12 Russian diplomats from the U.S. under claims of abuse of power.

On March 4, 2022, the United States and its allies strongly denounced Russia at the United Nations on Friday for shelling and seizing Europe's largest nuclear power facility overnight in Ukraine, and some insisted that Moscow not allow such an action to happen again.[^354]

On March 13, 2022, President Biden's National Security Adviser [Jake Sullivan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jake_Sullivan "Jake Sullivan") warned of a full-fledged [NATO](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NATO "NATO") response if Russia were to hit any part of NATO territory.[^355]

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Russian-list-of-unfriendly-countries.svg/330px-Russian-list-of-unfriendly-countries.svg.png)

The United States is on Russia's " Unfriendly Countries List " (red). Countries and territories on the list have imposed or joined sanctions against Russia. 356

The poll, conducted by [NPR](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NPR "NPR") / [Ipsos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ipsos "Ipsos") between March 18 and 21, 2022, found that only 36% of Americans approved the [Biden administration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Presidency_of_Joe_Biden "Presidency of Joe Biden") 's response to the invasion.[^357]

On April 28, 2022, President Biden asked Congress for an additional $33 billion to assist Ukraine, including $20 billion to provide [weapons to Ukraine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_military_aid_to_Ukraine_during_the_Russo-Ukrainian_War "List of military aid to Ukraine during the Russo-Ukrainian War").[^358]

On July 6, 2022, the speaker of the [Russian Parliament](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_Parliament "Russian Parliament") threatened the US about the possible " [return](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alaska_Purchase "Alaska Purchase") " of [Alaska](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alaska "Alaska") to [Russia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russia "Russia").[^359]

On September 21, 2022, President Putin warned the US and NATO during his [partial mobilization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2022_Russian_mobilization "2022 Russian mobilization") speech regarding Russia's ability to use nuclear weapons, stating that if Russia's "territorial integrity" was threatened, Russia would "certainly make use of all weapon systems available" to them.[^360]

On September 27, 2022, White House press secretary [Karine Jean-Pierre](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karine_Jean-Pierre "Karine Jean-Pierre") encouraged Russian men fleeing their home country to avoid being drafted to apply for [asylum in the United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asylum_in_the_United_States "Asylum in the United States").[^361] In early 2023, the Biden administration resumed deportations of [Russians who had fled Russia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_emigration_following_the_Russian_invasion_of_Ukraine "Russian emigration following the Russian invasion of Ukraine") due to mobilization and political persecution. Texas-based attorney Jennifer Scarborough said that "In March of 2022, the US said they were stopping deportations to Russia because of the political situation – so I don't understand why they restarted it and they did it so quietly." [^362]

On January 25, 2023, the Biden administration decided to supply 31 [M1 Abrams](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/M1_Abrams "M1 Abrams") tanks to Ukraine.[^363]

In February 2023, the [United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States "United States") stepped up efforts to pressure the countries, including [Turkey](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turkey "Turkey") and the [United Arab Emirates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates "United Arab Emirates"), to stop the commercial activities that had been benefiting Russia and helping them to evade international sanctions. Turkey, a [NATO](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NATO "NATO") member, and the United Arab Emirates, a close ally of the US, agreed to Western pressure and imposed sanctions on Russia.[^364]

A [Gallup](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gallup,_Inc. "Gallup, Inc.") poll conducted in June 2023 found that 62% of respondents in the United States wanted to [support Ukraine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_military_aid_to_Ukraine_during_the_Russo-Ukrainian_War "List of military aid to Ukraine during the Russo-Ukrainian War") in regaining territory that Russia had captured, even if it meant prolonging the war between Russia and Ukraine, while 32% wanted to end the war as quickly as possible, even if it meant allowing Russia to keep the territory it captured and [annexed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_annexation_of_Donetsk,_Kherson,_Luhansk_and_Zaporizhzhia_oblasts "Russian annexation of Donetsk, Kherson, Luhansk and Zaporizhzhia oblasts") in southeastern Ukraine.[^365] According to a 2023 CNN poll, 55% of American respondents said the US Congress should not approve additional funding to support Ukraine, while 45% would support additional funding.[^366]

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/M1A1_into_a_Poklonnaya_hill.jpg/250px-M1A1_into_a_Poklonnaya_hill.jpg)

A captured US-supplied M1 Abrams main battle tank in Ukrainian service on display at Moscow's Victory Park on Poklonnaya Hill, 2024

In 2022, Congress approved more than $112 billion to help Ukraine in its war with Russia. At the end of 2023, the White House requested $61.4 billion more for Ukraine for the year ahead.[^367]

In January 2024, the Biden administration rejected Vladimir Putin's proposal for a [ceasefire in Ukraine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peace_negotiations_in_the_Russian_invasion_of_Ukraine "Peace negotiations in the Russian invasion of Ukraine"). Biden's national security advisor [Jake Sullivan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jake_Sullivan "Jake Sullivan") informed Putin's foreign policy adviser [Yuri Ushakov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yuri_Ushakov "Yuri Ushakov") that the United States would not discuss a ceasefire without Ukraine's participation.[^368] [^369]

On April 13, 2024, the [London Metal Exchange](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/London_Metal_Exchange "London Metal Exchange") set out measures following US and UK imposed sanctions that banned delivery of new Russian metal including aluminum, copper and nickel. Aim of this decision is to agitate Russian export revenue as Moscow continues it war against Ukraine.[^370]

On May 30, 2024, Biden gave Ukraine permission to [strike targets inside Russia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Attacks_in_Russia_during_the_Russian_invasion_of_Ukraine "Attacks in Russia during the Russian invasion of Ukraine") near the [Kharkiv region](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kharkiv_Oblast "Kharkiv Oblast") using American-supplied weapons. The Russians were then exploiting the artificial limitation to focus attacks on the city, which lies 60 km from the frontier.[^371] A little later he sat with [David Muir](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/David_Muir "David Muir") during the D-Day memorial in Normandy France and said "They are authorized to be used in proximity to the border. We are not authorizing strikes 200 miles into Russia. We are not authorizing strikes on Moscow, on the Kremlin." [^372] On August 9, 2024, a convoy of Russian troops in the [Kursk Oblast](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kursk_Oblast "Kursk Oblast") of Russia was destroyed in a strike by U.S.-supplied [HIMARS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/M142_HIMARS "M142 HIMARS") rocket system in what the Russian Telegram channel called "one of the bloodiest and most massive strikes in the entire war." [^373] [^374]

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/2024_Russian_prisoner_exchange%2C_prisoners_en_route_back_to_the_United_StatesGT6hPjcWwAABuWv_%28cropped%29.jpg/250px-2024_Russian_prisoner_exchange%2C_prisoners_en_route_back_to_the_United_StatesGT6hPjcWwAABuWv_%28cropped%29.jpg)

Alsu Kurmasheva, Paul Whelan and Evan Gershkovich together with government officials and staff on their return flight to the U.S. on August 1, 2024

In June 2024, the U.S. sanctioned multiple [Hong Kong](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hong_Kong "Hong Kong") firms, for facilitating trade in gold produced by Russia's [Polyus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyus_\(company\) "Polyus (company)"). As per the Treasury Department, several Hong Kong and the [UAE](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates "United Arab Emirates") -based were used to convert payments from sale of Russian gold into fiat currency and cryptocurrency. Hong Kong's Holden International Trading Limited and Taube Precious HK Limited were used to route payments, while the UAE-based Red Coast Metals Trading DMCC to obscure payments from Russian gold sale. Besides, Hong Kong-based VPower Finance Security was transporting the Russian gold.[^375]

On July 28, 2024, Russian president Vladimir Putin threatened to deploy [long-range missiles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ballistic_missile "Ballistic missile") that could hit all of Europe after the United States announced its intention to deploy long-range missiles in [Germany](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Germany "Germany") starting in 2026 that could hit Russian territory within 10 minutes.[^376]

On August 1, 2024, the United States and Russia conducted the most extensive [prisoner exchange](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2024_Russian_prisoner_exchange "2024 Russian prisoner exchange") since the end of the [Cold War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War "Cold War"),[^377] involving the release of twenty-six people.

Since the Russian invasion of Ukraine, the [persecution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_rights_in_Russia "Human rights in Russia") of Russian citizens who disagree with the policies of Russian leader Vladimir Putin has increased significantly. For example, in early 2024, ballet dancer [Ksenia Karelina](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ksenia_Karelina "Ksenia Karelina"), a dual American-Russian citizen and resident of Los Angeles, was arrested while visiting family in Russia and charged with treason for sending $51.80 to [Razom](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Razom "Razom"), a [New York City](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_York_City "New York City") -based nonprofit organization that sends humanitarian assistance to [Ukraine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ukraine "Ukraine").[^378] She initially faced life in prison, but pleaded guilty and was sentenced to 12 years in prison.[^379]

### Second Trump administration (2025–present)

Russia has yet to observe any meaningful progress on nuclear disarmament from the new U.S. administration, as stated by Gennady Gatilov on February 9, 2025, Russia's Permanent Representative to the UN in Geneva. While expressing readiness for collaboration, Gatilov highlighted the lack of advancement in [nuclear arms control](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arms_control "Arms control") discussions, with the New START Treaty set to expire in 2026.[^380]

#### Talks on the Russo-Ukrainian War and normalizing relations

Donald Trump spoke to Vladimir Putin by telephone on February 12, 2025, in what was their first known contact since Trump had been [inaugurated](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Second_inauguration_of_Donald_Trump "Second inauguration of Donald Trump") for his second term as U.S. president.[^381] They discussed a range of topics, including opening negotiations to end the war and arranging a [summit meeting](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Summit_meeting "Summit meeting"). Trump announced afterwards that peace talks would begin "immediately" and that [U.S. Secretary of State](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/U.S._Secretary_of_State "U.S. Secretary of State") [Marco Rubio](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marco_Rubio "Marco Rubio"), [U.S. National Security Advisor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/U.S._National_Security_Advisor "U.S. National Security Advisor") [Mike Waltz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mike_Waltz "Mike Waltz"), [CIA Director](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Director_of_the_Central_Intelligence_Agency "Director of the Central Intelligence Agency") [John Ratcliffe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Ratcliffe_\(American_politician\) "John Ratcliffe (American politician)"), and Special Envoy [Steve Witkoff](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Steve_Witkoff "Steve Witkoff") will lead the negotiations.[^381] [^382] Trump also announced that he could meet with Putin in [Saudi Arabia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saudi_Arabia "Saudi Arabia"), and that [Saudi Crown Prince](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crown_Prince_of_Saudi_Arabia "Crown Prince of Saudi Arabia") [Mohammed bin Salman](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mohammed_bin_Salman "Mohammed bin Salman") could have a role in the talks, but no decision had been made at that time.[^381]

On February 15, Russian foreign minister Sergey Lavrov spoke to Marco Rubio by telephone about a number of topics, including the Russo-Ukrainian War, arranging a high-level meeting in Saudi Arabia, and potential cooperation on the [situation in the Middle East](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Middle_Eastern_crisis_\(2023%E2%80%93present\) "Middle Eastern crisis (2023–present)"). The conversation, which was the first contact between Russia and the U.S. at the foreign minister level in almost two years, was also seen as a sign that the two countries were restoring regular contact between their governments.[^383]

[Kremlin Press Secretary](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kremlin_Press_Secretary "Kremlin Press Secretary") [Dmitry Peskov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dmitry_Peskov "Dmitry Peskov") said on February 17 that the Riyadh talks will be about "restoring the entire range of U.S.-Russian relations, as well as preparing possible talks on the Ukrainian settlement and organizing a meeting of the two presidents." [Tammy Bruce](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tammy_Bruce "Tammy Bruce"), the [U.S. State Department Spokesperson](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spokesperson_for_the_United_States_Department_of_State "Spokesperson for the United States Department of State"), stated on the same day that the talks were to see if a potential war settlement was possible, in which case more detailed negotiations could move forward.[^384] Sergey Lavrov ruled out the participation of the [European Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Union "European Union") countries. He also said, speaking of the earlier phone call between Putin and Trump, that the two presidents "agreed to leave behind that absolutely abnormal period in relations between our great powers when they effectively halted any contacts except for some technical and humanitarian issues." [^384] Shortly before the talks, [Kirill Dmitriev](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kirill_Dmitriev "Kirill Dmitriev"), the head of [Russia's sovereign wealth fund](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_Direct_Investment_Fund "Russian Direct Investment Fund"), met with the U.S. delegation to discuss the economic relationship between their countries.[^385]

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Secretary_Rubio_Holds_a_Meeting_with_Saudi_Arabia_and_Russia_%2854334316072%29.jpg/250px-Secretary_Rubio_Holds_a_Meeting_with_Saudi_Arabia_and_Russia_%2854334316072%29.jpg)

U.S., Saudi, and Russian officials meeting in Riyadh, February 18, 2025

The [Riyadh meeting](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/February_2025_United_States%E2%80%93Russia_summit_in_Saudi_Arabia "February 2025 United States–Russia summit in Saudi Arabia") was held on February 18, with Rubio, Waltz, and Witkoff on the American side, and Lavrov and Presidential Aide [Yuri Ushakov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yuri_Ushakov "Yuri Ushakov") on the Russian side. Saudi Arabian foreign minister [Faisal bin Farhan Al Saud](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Faisal_bin_Farhan_Al_Saud "Faisal bin Farhan Al Saud") and National Security Adviser [Musaad bin Mohammed Al Aiban](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Musaad_bin_Mohammed_Al_Aiban "Musaad bin Mohammed Al Aiban") were also there. The delegations agreed to begin the [negotiation process for ending the war in Ukraine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peace_negotiations_in_the_Russian_invasion_of_Ukraine "Peace negotiations in the Russian invasion of Ukraine") (though the Ukrainians were not invited to the talks), to establish a high-level mechanism for contact between the U.S. and Russian governments, and to work to eventually normalize their economic and diplomatic ties.[^386] [^387] This will include restoring the staff of both of their embassies to normal levels, after the expulsions of diplomats in previous years.[^388] Lavrov said after the talks: "We weren't just listening to each other, but we heard each other. I have reason to believe that the American side started to better understand our positions." [^389]

Yuri Ushakov, the Russian presidential advisor on foreign policy, said after the Riyadh meeting that preparations for a Trump-Putin summit have begun, though no date had been decided yet.[^386] [^390] According to Kirill Dmitriev, they also discussed the possibility of restoring joint energy exploration projects in the Arctic, where [ExxonMobil](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ExxonMobil "ExxonMobil") previously had a partnership with [Rosneft](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rosneft "Rosneft") before leaving due to economic sanctions in 2018.[^391] The U.S.-Russia talks in Riyadh, their most extensive negotiation in three years,[^389] have been described as representing an "extraordinary turnaround" [^392] and a "head-spinning reset" in the relations between the two countries.[^389] Speaking at a press conference on the same day, Trump said that he will "probably" meet Putin later in February.[^393] Rubio said a potential meeting between Trump and Putin would "largely depend on whether we can make progress on ending the war in Ukraine." [^394]

Trump said on February 21 that Russia attacked Ukraine, but blamed Biden for failing to prevent it and Zelensky for abandoning peace talks.[^395] He also said that Zelenskyy and Putin should negotiate an end to the war to prevent further [killings of young soldiers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Casualties_of_the_Russo-Ukrainian_War "Casualties of the Russo-Ukrainian War") and the deaths of "millions" of people.[^394]

On February 24, the US voted against a [UN General Assembly resolution condemning Russia's invasion of Ukraine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly_Resolution_ES-11/7 "United Nations General Assembly Resolution ES-11/7").[^396] Alongside this resolution, the US [introduced a separate resolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly_Resolution_ES-11/8 "United Nations General Assembly Resolution ES-11/8") in the General Assembly which was worded in neutral terms. However, this resolution was significantly changed after several amendments, and Russia voted against it.[^397] The US later re-introduced that resolution in the Security Council, [which ultimately approved it](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_Resolution_2774 "United Nations Security Council Resolution 2774").[^398]

On February 27, Trump extended a series of [sanctions against Russia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_sanctions_during_the_Russo-Ukrainian_War "International sanctions during the Russo-Ukrainian War") over its invasion of Ukraine for one year.[^399]

On March 4, the media reported that the United States had suspended all military aid to Ukraine.[^400] Director of the CIA [John Ratcliffe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Ratcliffe "John Ratcliffe") confirmed this, along with the suspension of intelligence sharing with Ukraine.[^401] Aid and intelligence sharing was resumed on March 11 following talks with Ukrainian officials in [Jeddah](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jeddah "Jeddah"), Saudi Arabia.[^402]

In June 2025, a majority of [U.S. senators](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/119th_United_States_Congress "119th United States Congress") supported [secondary sanctions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Secondary_sanctions "Secondary sanctions") against Russia that would impose 500% [tariffs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tariffs_in_the_second_Trump_administration "Tariffs in the second Trump administration") on countries that buy [Russian oil](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Petroleum_industry_in_Russia "Petroleum industry in Russia"), [natural gas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural_gas_in_Russia "Natural gas in Russia"), [uranium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mining_industry_of_Russia "Mining industry of Russia") and other exports. Majority Leader [John Thune](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Thune "John Thune") said senators "stand ready to provide President Trump with any tools he needs to get Russia to finally come to the table in a real way." [^403]

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/P20250815DT-0831_President_Donald_Trump_meets_with_Russian_President_Vladimir_Putin_at_Joint_Base_Elmendorf_Richardson_in_Anchorage%2C_Alaska.jpg/250px-P20250815DT-0831_President_Donald_Trump_meets_with_Russian_President_Vladimir_Putin_at_Joint_Base_Elmendorf_Richardson_in_Anchorage%2C_Alaska.jpg)

US president Donald Trump, Russian president Vladimir Putin and Russian foreign minister Sergey Lavrov at the summit meeting in Alaska, August 2025

In June 2025, Trump rejected Putin's offer to mediate a ceasefire in the [Iran–Israel war](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Twelve-Day_War "Twelve-Day War"),[^404] telling him to focus on mediating a ceasefire in the Russo-Ukrainian War.[^405] On June 22, 2025, Putin condemned Trump's [strikes on Iranian nuclear sites](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_strikes_on_Iranian_nuclear_sites "United States strikes on Iranian nuclear sites") as an "unprovoked act of aggression".[^406]

A [summit meeting](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2025_Russia%E2%80%93United_States_Summit_in_Alaska "2025 Russia–United States Summit in Alaska") took place on August 15, 2025, in the U.S. [state of Alaska](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alaska "Alaska"), between President of the United States Donald Trump and President of Russia Vladimir Putin. The meeting, announced by Trump on August 8, 2025, focused on negotiations toward a ceasefire and potential peace agreement in the ongoing [Russo–Ukrainian War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russo-Ukrainian_War "Russo-Ukrainian War"). Trump suggested that the talks could include proposals involving territorial adjustments, an idea publicly rejected by Ukrainian president [Volodymyr Zelenskyy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Volodymyr_Zelenskyy "Volodymyr Zelenskyy"), who insisted that no settlement can occur without Ukraine's participation or territorial concessions.[^407] [^408] This was the first high-level bilateral meeting between the two countries on U.S. soil since the [1988 Governors Island Summit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Governors_Island_Summit "Governors Island Summit"). Alaska was selected in part due to its historical ties to Russia and because it lies outside the jurisdiction of the International Criminal Court, which has issued an arrest warrant for Putin.[^409] The summit sparked controversy over its agenda, format, and symbolism. Central to the dispute was President Trump's suggestion of potential territorial "swaps" as part of a [Ukraine peace deal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peace_negotiations_in_the_Russian_invasion_of_Ukraine "Peace negotiations in the Russian invasion of Ukraine").[^409]

In late August 2025, as U.S.-Russia relations showed signs of renewed engagement, both leaders expressed interest in expanded economic cooperation. Concurrently, [ExxonMobil](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ExxonMobil "ExxonMobil") and Russia's state-owned [Rosneft](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rosneft "Rosneft") had secretly outlined a roadmap for resuming joint operations at the [Sakhalin-1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sakhalin-I "Sakhalin-I") oil and gas project, contingent on government approvals and progress in Ukraine peace negotiations. Exxon Senior Vice President Neil Chapman led the discussions with Rosneft CEO [Igor Sechin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Igor_Sechin "Igor Sechin"), while Exxon also sought support from the U.S. government for its potential return. According to officials, the company received a favorable response, and CEO [Darren Woods](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Darren_Woods "Darren Woods") held recent talks with President Trump at the White House regarding the matter.[^410]

On October 22, 2025, the United States imposed sanctions on Russian energy companies Rosneft and [Lukoil](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lukoil "Lukoil"),[^411] affecting their customers in [China](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/China_and_the_Russo-Ukrainian_War "China and the Russo-Ukrainian War") and [India](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/India_and_the_Russian_invasion_of_Ukraine "India and the Russian invasion of Ukraine").[^412]

Between February 28 and March 1, 2026, relations between the United States and Russia deteriorated again after the [massive attack on Iran](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2026_Israeli%E2%80%93United_States_strikes_on_Iran "2026 Israeli–United States strikes on Iran") led by Trump and Israeli Prime Minister [Netanyahu](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Benjamin_Netanyahu "Benjamin Netanyahu"), which led to [Ali Khamenei's death](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Assassination_of_Ali_Khamenei "Assassination of Ali Khamenei"), Iran's supreme leader since 1989.[^413] [^414]

During the conflict Russia provided Iran with intelligence and improved drone technology to target U.S. military assets in the Middle East.[^415] [^416]

On a call on March 9, Trump rejected Putin’s offer to mediate a ceasefire between the United States and Iran and a proposal for Russia to house the latter’s enriched uranium. In a press conference after the call, he said, ”\[Putin\] wants to be helpful \[with Iran\]. I told him you can be more helpful by ending the war in Ukraine.” [^417] [^418] [^419]

During ceasefire talks on March 12 concerning the [Russo-Ukrainian war](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russo-Ukrainian_war_\(2022%E2%80%93present\) "Russo-Ukrainian war (2022–present)") in Miami, Florida, special presidential envoy Dimitriev offered that Russia wouldn’t provide military intelligence to Iran if the United States did the same for Ukraine which Witkoff and Jared Kushner rejected.[^419] [^420]

## Russian and U.S. intelligence operations

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/RIAN_archive_793499_Exhibition_of_remains_of_U.S._U-2_spy-in-the-sky_aircraft.jpg/250px-RIAN_archive_793499_Exhibition_of_remains_of_U.S._U-2_spy-in-the-sky_aircraft.jpg)

Military attaches of foreign embassies visiting the exhibition of remains of U.S. U-2 reconnaissance aircraft destroyed on May 1, 1960, near Sverdlovsk (now Yekaterinburg ).

The Soviet Union's systemic espionage efforts in the U.S. began in the 1920s.[^421]

In April 2015, CNN reported that "Russian hackers" had "penetrated sensitive parts of the White House" computers in "recent months." It was said that the FBI, the Secret Service, and other U.S. intelligence agencies categorized the attacks "among the most sophisticated attacks ever launched against U.S. government systems." [^422]

In 2017, a cybersecurity specialist working in the [Federal Security Service](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Federal_Security_Service "Federal Security Service") was arrested by Russian authorities on suspicion of passing information to U.S. intelligence.[^423]

In June 2019, Russia said that its [electrical grid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electricity_sector_in_Russia "Electricity sector in Russia") had come under cyber-attack by the United States. The *New York Times* reported that American hackers from the [United States Cyber Command](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Cyber_Command "United States Cyber Command") planted malware potentially capable of disrupting the Russian electrical grid.[^424]

US intelligence identified a fake video, allegedly produced by Russian operatives, showing individuals falsely claiming to commit voter fraud in Georgia. Aimed at creating distrust in US elections, the video prompted Georgia's election chief to request its removal from social media platforms.[^425]

## Mutual perceptions by the countries' populations

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Barack_Obama_at_the_New_Economic_School_graduation.jpg/250px-Barack_Obama_at_the_New_Economic_School_graduation.jpg)

President Obama greets attendees at the New Economic School graduation in Gostinny Dvor, Moscow, July 7, 2009

A poll by the [University of Maryland, College Park](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Maryland,_College_Park "University of Maryland, College Park"), released early July 2009 found that only 2 percent of Russians had "a lot of confidence" that U.S. president [Barack Obama](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barack_Obama "Barack Obama") would do the right thing in world affairs.[^426] Russian media criticized the United States for pursuing an [anti-missile system in Europe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_missile_defense#Recent_developments "National missile defense"), for favoring [NATO expansion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Enlargement_of_NATO "Enlargement of NATO") and for supporting [Georgia in its armed conflict with Russia in 2008](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russo-Georgian_War "Russo-Georgian War").[^427]

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/History_of_NATO_enlargement.svg/250px-History_of_NATO_enlargement.svg.png)

Russians have criticized the United States over the past years for favoring NATO's eastward expansion. 428

Prior to 2014, the Russian press expressed varying opinions of Russia–United States relations.[^429] Russian media treatment of America ranged from doctrinaire [^430] and nationalistic [^431] to very positive toward the United States and the West.[^432] [^433] [^434] [^435] In 2013, 51 percent of Russians had a favorable view of the U.S., down from 57 percent in 2010.[^436]

The opinion polls taken by the independent [Levada Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Levada_Center "Levada Center") in January 2015,[^437] showed 81 percent of Russians tended to hold negative views of the U.S., a number that had nearly doubled over the previous 12 months and that was by far the highest negative rating since the center started tracking those views in 1988, as well as surpassing any time since the [Stalin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stalin "Stalin") era, according to observers.[^438] This contrasts with only 7 percent of Russians in April 1990 who said they had bad or somewhat bad attitudes towards the U.S.[^439] Likewise, the figures published by [Gallup](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gallup_\(company\) "Gallup (company)") in February 2015 showed a significant rise in anti-Russian sentiment in the U.S.: the proportion of Americans who considered Russia as a "critical military threat" had over the 12 months increased from 32 to 49 percent, and, for the first time in many years, Russia topped the list of America's perceived external enemies, ahead of North Korea, China and Iran, with 18 percent of U.S. residents putting Russia at the top of the list of the "United States' greatest enemy today".[^440] Public opinion polls taken by the [Pew Research Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pew_Research_Center "Pew Research Center") showed that favorable U.S. public opinion of Russia was at 22 percent in 2015. The most negative view of Russia was at 19 percent in 2014, and the most positive view at 49 percent in 2010 and 2011.[^441] The most negative view of the United States was at 15 percent in 2015, while the most positive view was at 61 percent in 2002.[^442]

US public opinion regarding Russia has changed substantially over the past 25 years. A Gallup poll from 1992 to 2017 shows 62% of American respondents having a favorable view of Russia in 1992, and 29% having an unfavorable view. In 2017, 70% of American respondents had an unfavorable view of Russia, and 28% had a favorable view.[^443] A February 2023 Gallup poll found that 9% of Americans have a favorable view of Russia, and 51% view the military power of Russia as a critical threat, though this is down significantly from 59% a year prior.[^444] A poll conducted by [YouGov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/YouGov "YouGov") in 2015 found that only 11% of Americans believed that the Soviet Union contributed most to the [defeat of Nazi Germany](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eastern_Front_\(World_War_II\) "Eastern Front (World War II)") in [World War II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_War_II "World War II").[^445]

A 2017 survey conducted by the [Pew Research Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pew_Research_Center "Pew Research Center") showed 41% of Russians had a positive view of the US, only one of two countries surveyed where positive perception for the US increased; 52% expressed a negative view.[^446] The same study also showed 53% of Russians had confidence in the U.S. president [Donald Trump](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Donald_Trump "Donald Trump"), compared to just 11% for former president [Barack Obama](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barack_Obama "Barack Obama").[^447]

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Fear_Factory_in_Saint_Petersburg_2013-7.jpg/250px-Fear_Factory_in_Saint_Petersburg_2013-7.jpg)

American metal band Fear Factory in Saint Petersburg.

There has also been a change in whether Americans view Russia as an ally or a threat. In 1992, 44% of American respondents saw Russia to be friendly but not an ally, and 5% see them as a threat. In 2014, the Gallup poll reports that 21% of Americans see Russia as friendly but not an ally, and 24% of American respondents seeing them as a threat.[^443] This difference in how Americans view Russia has been attributed to the increasing lack of cooperation in the scientific field between the US and Russia, by some.[^448] Another perspective is the shift from ally to threat is due to the US being critical of Russia's aggression, especially with their aggression towards geographic neighbors,[^449] the United States being one of those neighbors, as it shares a common sea border with the Russian Federation and the US [State of Alaska](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/State_of_Alaska "State of Alaska").

The 2016 surveys independently conducted by the [Chicago Council](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chicago_Council_of_Global_Affairs "Chicago Council of Global Affairs") and Russia's [Levada Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Levada_Center "Levada Center") showed that mutual perceptions between Russians and Americans were at levels not seen since the Cold War, indicating considerable mutual distrust.[^450]

U.S.–Russian relations have further deteriorated since 2016.[^451] A December 2017 survey conducted by the Chicago Council and its Russian partner, the Levada Center, showed that:

> Seventy-eight percent of Russians polled said the United States meddles "a great deal" or "a fair amount" in Russian politics, compared to 69 percent of Americans who say the same about Russian interference in U.S. politics.... The poll found that 31 percent of Russians said Moscow tried to influence U.S. domestic affairs in a significant way, compared to 55 percent of Americans who felt that their own government tried to do the same thing in Russia.... Only 31 percent of Americans say they hold a positive view of Russia, and 24 percent of Russians say the same of the United States.... Eighty-one percent of Russians said they felt the United States was working to undermine Russia on the world stage; 77 percent of Americans said the same of Russia.[^452]

A Levada poll released in August 2018 found that 68% of Russian respondents believe that Russia needs to dramatically improve relations with the United States and other Western countries.[^453] According to *The Moscow Times*, "Russians increasingly view the United States in a positive light following a presidential" [summit in Helsinki](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2018_Russia%E2%80%93United_States_Summit "2018 Russia–United States Summit") in July 2018. "For the first time since 2014, the number of Russians who said they had "positive" feelings towards the United States (42 percent) outweighed those who reported "negative" feelings (40 percent)." [^454]

The 2019 poll independently conducted by the Chicago Council and Levada Center found that 85% of Russians and 78% of Americans say the United States and Russia are "more rivals than partners." [^455] The president of the [Center for Citizen Initiatives](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Center_for_Citizen_Initiatives "Center for Citizen Initiatives"), [Sharon Tennison](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Sharon_Tennison&action=edit&redlink=1 "Sharon Tennison (page does not exist)"), stated in 2019, "In my 35 years of traveling throughout Russia, I've never before witnessed such a vast gap between what average Americans 'believe' about Russia and Russia's reality on ground today." [^455]

A Levada poll released in February 2020 found that 80% of Russian respondents believe that Russia and the West should become friends and partners.[^456] However, only 42% of Russians polled said they had a positive view of the United States.[^456] Only 18% of Americans polled by [Pew Research Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pew_Research_Center "Pew Research Center") said they had a positive view of Russia.[^457] According to the [Pew Research Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pew_Research_Center "Pew Research Center"), "57% of Russians ages 18 to 29 see the U.S. favorably, compared with only 15% of Russians ages 50 and older." [^458] In 2019, only 20% of Russians viewed U.S. president [Donald Trump](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Donald_Trump "Donald Trump") positively.[^459] Only 14% of Russians expressed net approval of [Donald Trump's foreign policies and actions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Foreign_policy_of_the_first_Donald_Trump_administration "Foreign policy of the first Donald Trump administration").[^460]
