A 2024 Gallup poll shows that twenty-six percent of Americans consider Russia to be the United States' greatest enemy today, ranking it as the second-largest known enemy of the United States after China.[^461] According to a 2026 Gallup poll, only 17% of Americans have favorable opinions of North Korea, while 79% have an unfavorable opinion. This was the third most negative ranking Americans gave out of the 21 foreign countries surveyed.[^462]

### Propaganda

- The U.S. government funds [Radio Free Europe/Radio Liberty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radio_Free_Europe/Radio_Liberty "Radio Free Europe/Radio Liberty") that broadcasts in 26 languages to many countries.[^463] The radio's broadcasting is viewed by Russian researchers as an instrument of American propaganda targeting Russia as a state.[^464] According to *[The Intercept](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Intercept "The Intercept")*, some American media have been accused of spreading anti-Russian propaganda." [^465] [^466] [^467]
- Russia funds [Russia Today](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RT_\(TV_network\) "RT (TV network)") and [Sputnik News](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sputnik_\(news_agency\) "Sputnik (news agency)") which have been accused of pushing pro-Kremlin narratives internationally. In 2021, the Russian state media budget was 211 billion rubles (about $2.8 billion USD), an increase of 34 billion-ruble ($460 million USD) over previous years.[^468] According to a [University of Oxford](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Oxford "University of Oxford") report, Moscow uses RT "to sow conspiracy theories to cast doubt on traditional media outlets" and "skewing news output to promote narratives that showed the West as corrupt, divided and out of touch." [^469] The influence operation also extends to US allies. RT and Sputnik were cited by the European Parliament's resolution of November 23, 2016, as the Russian government's tools of "propaganda against the EU and its North American partners" such as pushing narratives against democratic values and portraying eastern countries as failed states.[^470] The [RT America](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RT_America "RT America") network has employed Americans, including TV hosts and [political commentators](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Political_commentator "Political commentator") such as [Larry King](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Larry_King "Larry King") and [Ed Schultz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ed_Schultz "Ed Schultz"), to help them appear more like a legitimate outlet. [Jim Rutenberg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jim_Rutenberg "Jim Rutenberg") described them "wittingly or not... playing the equestrians to Russia's trojan horse." [^471] [^472]

## Timeline of relations between the United States and Russia

The timeline covers key events, 1991 to present.[^473] [^474]

### Yeltsin era, 1991–99

- 1991: U.S. president [George H. W. Bush](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_H._W._Bush "George H. W. Bush") and USSR president [Mikhail Gorbachev](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mikhail_Gorbachev "Mikhail Gorbachev") sign [START I](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/START_I "START I") treaty, July 31.
- 1991: August: Soviet hardliners stage a coup against Gorbachev; they fail because of defiance by Russian president [Boris Yeltsin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boris_Yeltsin "Boris Yeltsin"). Communism collapses overnight in the USSR.
- 1991: Gorbachev announces the dissolution of the USSR into 15 independent republics; Russia is the successor state to the USSR.
- 1992: Russian president Yeltsin visits the U.S. on January 26. He and Bush set up the [United States–Russia Joint Commission on P.O.W./M.I.A.'s](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/U.S.%E2%80%93Russia_Joint_Commission_on_POW/MIAs "U.S.–Russia Joint Commission on POW/MIAs"). Its mission is to discover what happened to POWs and those missing in action during the Cold War, as well as planes shot down, missing submarines. The committee had access to classified archives from the FBI and the KGB.[^475]
- 1992: The [Lisbon Protocol](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lisbon_Protocol "Lisbon Protocol") calls for the denuclearization of Ukraine, Belarus, Kazakhstan. May 23.[^476]
- 1992: Russia attends the Washington Summit on June 16.
- 1992: The United States and Russia sign an Agreement Concerning Cooperation in the Exploration and Use of Outer Space for Peaceful Purposes on June 17.[^477]
- 1993: Bush and Yeltsin sign the [START II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/START_II "START II") treaty in Moscow on January 3.[^478]
- 1993: First summit meeting between U.S. president [Bill Clinton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bill_Clinton "Bill Clinton") and Yeltsin on April 4 in Vancouver, Canada, to discuss a new and expanded $1 billion aid package intended to support Russian democrats and to fund medical supplies, food and grain assistance as well as loans to Russian entrepreneurs.[^479]
- 1993: The U.S. announces a bilateral aid program of $1.8 billion for Russia and the former Soviet republics on July 9 to 10.
- 1993: The U.S.–Russian Commission on technical cooperation in energy and space has its first meeting in Washington, D.C., on August 31 to September 2.
- 1994: Presidents Clinton and Yeltsin sign the [Kremlin accords](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kremlin_accords "Kremlin accords") on January 14 in Moscow.
- 1994: First joint U.S.–Russia Space Shuttle mission on February 3.
- 1994: The United States and Russia move to end the practice of aiming their strategic nuclear missiles at each other on May 30.
- 1994: Russia joins the [Partnership for Peace](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Partnership_for_Peace "Partnership for Peace") program on June 22.
- 1995: Presidents Clinton and Yeltsin hold a summit on European Security in Moscow on May 9 to 10.
- 1995: Russia joins the NATO-led [IFOR](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Implementation_Force "Implementation Force") in the aftermath of the [Bosnian War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bosnian_War "Bosnian War") on December 20.
- 1996: Ratification of [START II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/START_II "START II") treaty on January 26.
- 1996: Clinton and Yeltsin attend the Summit of the Peacemakers in Sharm al-Sheikh, Egypt to condemn the terrorist attacks in Israel and to declare their support for the Middle East peace process on March 14.
- 1996: Clinton attends a Summit on Nuclear Safety and Security with Yeltsin in Moscow on April 20.
- 1997: Russia joins the NATO-led [Euro-Atlantic Partnership Council](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euro-Atlantic_Partnership_Council "Euro-Atlantic Partnership Council") to cooperate on political and security issues on January 1.
- 1997: Clinton and Yeltsin hold another summit on European Security in Helsinki, Finland, on March 21. They reach some economic agreements, but there is continued disagreement on NATO expansion.
- 1997: April. Moscow summit with [General Secretary of the Chinese Communist Party](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/General_Secretary_of_the_Chinese_Communist_Party "General Secretary of the Chinese Communist Party") [Jiang Zemin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jiang_Zemin "Jiang Zemin") disapproves of American world domination; agree to reduce troops along Russia-China border.[^480]
- 1997: Russia attends the NATO summit in Paris, France, on May 27.
- 1997: The NATO-Russia Founding Act provides the formal basis of bilateral cooperation between the U.S., Russia and NATO is signed on May 27. Allows participation in NATO decision making; Russia agrees to drop opposition to NATO expansion in Central Europe.[^481]
- 1997: Russia joins the [G8](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/G8 "G8") at the [23rd G8 summit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/23rd_G8_summit "23rd G8 summit") in Denver, Colorado, on June 20 to 22.[^482]
- 1998: Clinton and Yeltsin agree to exchange information on missile launchings and to remove 50 metric tons of plutonium from their countries' nuclear weapons stocks in a summit in Moscow on September 1 to 2.
- 1999: Russia joins the NATO-led [KFOR](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kosovo_Force "Kosovo Force") in the aftermath of the [Kosovo War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kosovo_War "Kosovo War") on June 12.
- 1999: March: [Operation Allied Force](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Operation_Allied_Force "Operation Allied Force"): NATO bombing of Yugoslavia to force it out of Kosovo. Moscow attacked it as a breach of international law and a challenge to Russia's status in the Balkans.[^483]
- 1999: Clinton and Yeltsin meet at an Organization for Security Cooperation in Europe Summit Meeting in Istanbul, Turkey, from November 18–19, to discuss arms control, Chechnya and events in Europe. Clinton remarks that the international community does not dispute Russia's right to defend its territorial integrity and to fight terrorism.[^484]
![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Vladimir_Putin_in_the_United_States_13-16_November_2001-55.jpg/250px-Vladimir_Putin_in_the_United_States_13-16_November_2001-55.jpg)

Vladimir Putin and wife Lyudmila at service for victims of the September 11 attacks, November 16, 2001.

### Putin era, 2000–present

- 2000: Clinton visits Moscow to meet with new Russian president [Vladimir Putin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Putin "Vladimir Putin") on June 3 to 5.
- 2000: Clinton and Putin meet at the [United Nations Millennium Summit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Millennium_Summit "Millennium Summit") in New York City to call a plea for world peace on September 6.
- 2001: President [George W. Bush](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_W._Bush "George W. Bush") has a very friendly meeting with Putin at the [Slovenia summit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Slovenia_Summit_2001 "Slovenia Summit 2001") on June 16. At the closing press conference, Bush said: "I looked the man in the eye. I found him very straightforward and trustworthy – I was able to get a sense of his soul." Bush's top security aide [Condoleezza Rice](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Condoleezza_Rice "Condoleezza Rice") realized that Bush's phrasing had been a serious mistake. "We were never able to escape the perception that the president had naïvely trusted Putin and then been betrayed." [^485]
- 2001: Russia supports the U.S. in the aftermath of the [September 11 attacks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/September_11_attacks "September 11 attacks") on September 12.[^486]
- 2001: Russia opens a military hospital in Kabul, Afghanistan, to help the NATO military forces and Afghan civilians on December 2.
- 2002: Bush and Putin meet in Moscow and sign the [Strategic Offensive Reductions Treaty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Strategic_Offensive_Reductions_Treaty "Strategic Offensive Reductions Treaty") and declaration on a new strategic relationship between the U.S. and Russia on May 24.[^487]
- 2002: NATO and Russia create the NATO-Russia Council during [Rome summit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2002_Rome_summit "2002 Rome summit") on May 28.[^488]
- 2003: The "Roadmap for Peace" proposal developed by the U.S. in cooperation with Russia, the European Union, and the United Nations (the Quartet), was presented to Israel and the Palestinian Authority on April 30.[^489]
- 2003: Russia strongly condemns the United States in the lead-up to the [2003 invasion of Iraq](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2003_invasion_of_Iraq "2003 invasion of Iraq"), and calls for a peaceful solution to the crisis.[^490]![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Defense.gov_News_Photo_020313-D-2987S-016.jpg/250px-Defense.gov_News_Photo_020313-D-2987S-016.jpg)
	Donald Rumsfeld with Russian Minister of Defense Sergei Ivanov on March 13, 2002
- 2004: Bush gives condolences to Putin in the aftermath of the [Beslan school hostage crisis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Beslan_school_hostage_crisis "Beslan school hostage crisis") on September 21.
- 2006: Bush and Putin jointly announced the organization of the Global Initiative to Combat Nuclear Terrorism on July 16.[^491]
- 2006: The U.S. and Russia condemn North Korea's first nuclear launch test on October 6.
- 2008: Russian president [Dmitry Medvedev](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dmitry_Medvedev "Dmitry Medvedev") visits the U.S. for the first time at the [2008 G-20 summit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2008_G-20_Washington_summit "2008 G-20 Washington summit") in Washington, D.C., from November 14 to 15.
- 2009: February: US vice president Joe Biden suggests the new Obama administration would like to "reset" America's relationship with Russia, which had deteriorated to its lowest point since the Cold War after Russia's war with Georgia in 2008.[^492]
- 2009: Newly elected president [Barack Obama](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barack_Obama "Barack Obama") and Medvedev meet for the first time at the [G-20 Summit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2009_G-20_London_Summit "2009 G-20 London Summit") in London on April 1; they pledge to "deepen cooperation" on issues like nuclear terrorism.[^492]
- 2009: The U.S. and Russia disapprove the nuclear test by North Korea on May 25.[^493]
- 2009: Obama and Medvedev announce the [Obama–Medvedev Commission](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Obama%E2%80%93Medvedev_Commission "Obama–Medvedev Commission") to improve communication and cooperation between the U.S. and Russia in Moscow on July 6.
- 2009: U.S. chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff Admiral [Michael Mullen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Michael_Mullen "Michael Mullen") and Russian chief of the general staff [Nikolay Makarov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nikolay_Yegorovich_Makarov "Nikolay Yegorovich Makarov") sign a new strategic framework for military-to-military engagement between the U.S. and Russia on July 7.
- 2009: Obama administration cancels the eastern European missile defense program denounced by Russia.[^494]
- 2009: Russia agrees to allow U.S. and NATO troops and supplies to pass through Russia en route to Afghanistan on December 16.
- 2010: Obama and Medvedev sign [New START](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_START "New START") treaty in Prague, Czech Republic, to replace the [START I](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/START_I "START I") and it will eventually see the reduction of both nations' nuclear arsenals to 1,500 warheads for both the U.S. and Russia on April 8.![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Barack_Obama_and_Prime_Minister_Vladimir_Putin.jpg/250px-Barack_Obama_and_Prime_Minister_Vladimir_Putin.jpg)
	Barack Obama meets with Prime Minister Putin outside Moscow, July 7, 2009
- 2010: The U.S. and Russia call for Iran to give up on its nuclear weapons program along with the United Kingdom, France and China on June 9.
- 2010: Obama and Medvedev sign the " [New START](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_START "New START") " (New Strategic Arms Reduction Treaty). Goal is to reduce the deployed nuclear warheads on both sides by roughly 30 percent, down to 1,550. The treaty also limits the number of nuclear-armed submarines and bombers. New START went into force in February 2011.[^492]
- 2010: The U.S. and Russia conduct a joint anti-hijacking exercise called Vigilant Eagle-2010 on August 14.
- 2010: Foreign ministers from the U.S., Russia and NATO meet in New York to discuss areas of cooperation like Afghanistan, fighting piracy and combatting terrorism as well as ways of enhancing security within Europe on September 22.
- 2010: Medvedev attends the [2010 NATO summit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2010_Lisbon_summit "2010 Lisbon summit") in Portugal, from November 19 to 20. The U.S., Russia and NATO agree to cooperate on missile defense and other security issues as well as allowing more supplies for the U.S. and NATO to pass through Russia en route to Afghanistan as well as supplying [Afghan armed forces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghan_Armed_Forces "Afghan Armed Forces") with helicopters.
- 2011: The [New START](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_START "New START") treaty is ratified in Munich, Germany, by U.S. secretary of state [Hillary Clinton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hillary_Clinton "Hillary Clinton") and Russian foreign minister [Sergey Lavrov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sergey_Lavrov "Sergey Lavrov") on February 5.
- 2011: Ministers from the U.S., Russia and NATO meet in Berlin, Germany to discuss the situation in Libya and Afghanistan, as well as ongoing work on outlining the future framework for missile defence cooperation between the U.S., Russia and NATO on April 15.
- 2011: Russia congratulates the U.S. on the [killing of Osama bin Laden](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Death_of_Osama_bin_Laden "Death of Osama bin Laden") on May 2.
- 2011–present: [Syrian Civil War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Syrian_Civil_War "Syrian Civil War"); the government receives technical, financial, military and political support from Russia, while the U.S. favors some of the rebels. Russia provides diplomatic support in the United Nations as well. Russia has an interest in a military presence in the region, and in suppressing its own Muslim militants. It also rejects regime change imposed by the West.[^495]
- 2011: American, Russian and NATO ambassadors meet in Sochi, Russia, to restate their commitment to pursuing cooperation on missile defense as well as cooperation in other security areas of common interest on July 4.![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Lavrov_and_Clinton_in_Munich-1.jpg/250px-Lavrov_and_Clinton_in_Munich-1.jpg)
	U.S. secretary of state Hillary Clinton and Russian foreign minister Sergey Lavrov shake hands after signing the New START Treaty, Munich, Germany, on February 5, 2011
- 2011: American, Russian and NATO diplomats meet in New York to announce they have made progress in combating terrorism and enhancing Afghan transit on September 22.
- 2012: Russia agrees to host a U.S. and NATO transit hub at [Ulyanovsk airport](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ulyanovsk_Vostochny_Airport "Ulyanovsk Vostochny Airport") to help the U.S. and NATO withdrawal from Afghanistan in 2014 on March 21.
- 2012: Obama and Medvedev meet at the [2012 Nuclear Security Summit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2012_Nuclear_Security_Summit "2012 Nuclear Security Summit") in Seoul to discuss the increase economic trade on March 26.
- 2012: The U.S., Russia and NATO hold missile defense exercises in Germany, from March 26 to 30.
- 2012: American, Russian and NATO military forces agree to strengthen cooperation to counter piracy in the Horn of Africa on March 27.
- 2012: Russian prime minister [Dmitry Medvedev](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dmitry_Medvedev "Dmitry Medvedev") attends the [38th G8 summit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/38th_G8_summit "38th G8 summit") in Maryland, from May 18 to 19.
- 2012: Russia joins the U.S. and NATO at the [Chicago Summit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2012_Chicago_Summit "2012 Chicago Summit") on May 20.
- 2012: Obama and Putin meet at the [7th G-20 meeting](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2012_G-20_Los_Cabos_summit "2012 G-20 Los Cabos summit") in Los Cabos, Mexico, and call for an end to the [Syrian civil war](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Syrian_civil_war "Syrian civil war") on June 18 to 19.
- 2012: American and Russian navies participate in the [RIMPAC 2012](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exercise_RIMPAC "Exercise RIMPAC") naval exercises from June 29 to August 3.
- 2012: Russia joins the [WTO](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_Trade_Organization "World Trade Organization") and begins trade with the U.S. on August 22.[^496]
- 2013: Russia supports the U.S. against North Korea for North Korea building up tensions in the Korean peninsula and for threatening the U.S. during the [crisis with North Korea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2013_Korean_crisis "2013 Korean crisis") on April 8.
- 2013: The U.S. and Russia agree to intensify their cooperation in countering terrorism, including information exchange between intelligence organizations and conduct joint counter-terrorist operations as well as signing a cyber security pact to reduce the risk of conflict in cyberspace and signing the New Anti-Proliferation Deal in order to protect, control and account for nuclear materials on June 17 during the [39th G8 summit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/39th_G8_summit "39th G8 summit").![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Barack_Obama_and_Vladimir_Putin_walking_in_Ireland.jpg/250px-Barack_Obama_and_Vladimir_Putin_walking_in_Ireland.jpg)
	Obama at a bilateral meeting with Putin during the G8 summit in Ireland, June 17, 2013.
![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Cropped_Barack_Obama_and_Vladimir_Putin_shake_hands_at_G8_summit%2C_2013.jpg/250px-Cropped_Barack_Obama_and_Vladimir_Putin_shake_hands_at_G8_summit%2C_2013.jpg)

Putin and Obama shake hands at G8 summit, June 17, 2013

- 2013 August 7. President Obama cancels an upcoming summit with Putin; journalists call it "a rare, deliberate snub that reflects the fresh damage done by the Edward Snowden case to an important relationship already in decline." [^497]
- 2013: Obama and Putin make progress on the discussion of Syria at the end of the [2013 G-20 summit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2013_G-20_Saint_Petersburg_summit "2013 G-20 Saint Petersburg summit") in Saint Petersburg, Russia, on September 6.
- 2013: U.S. secretary of state [John Kerry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Kerry "John Kerry") and Russian foreign minister [Sergey Lavrov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sergey_Lavrov "Sergey Lavrov") meet in Geneva, Switzerland, and agree to secure and destroy Syria's chemical weapons on September 14.
- 2013: The U.S. and Russia along with the United Kingdom, France, China and Germany sign a deal with Iran about their nuclear program in Geneva, Switzerland, on November 27.
- 2014: The [Geneva II Conference](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geneva_II_Conference_on_Syria "Geneva II Conference on Syria")
- 2014: The U.S. Olympics team arrives in [Sochi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sochi "Sochi"), Russia, to participate in the [2014 Winter Olympics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2014_Winter_Olympics "2014 Winter Olympics") on January 30.
- 2014 – Continuing. see [Russian military intervention in Ukraine (2014–present)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_military_intervention_in_Ukraine_\(2014%E2%80%93present\) "Russian military intervention in Ukraine (2014–present)")
- 2014: The U.S. and Russia along with the [European Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Union "European Union") and Ukraine talk in Geneva about the crisis in Ukraine and reach an agreement to end the crisis on April 17.
- 2014: The U.S. and Russia start sending aid to Iraq to help fight [ISIS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islamic_State_of_Iraq_and_the_Levant "Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant") on June 5.
- 2015: The U.S. and Russia along with members of the [European Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Union "European Union") and Ukraine welcome [the new Minsk agreement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Minsk_agreements#Minsk_II,_February_2015 "Minsk agreements") to stop the [war in Donbas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/War_in_Donbas_\(2014%E2%80%932022\) "War in Donbas (2014–2022)") in Donbas on February 12.
- 2015: The U.S. and Russia agree to build a new space station to replace the [International Space Station](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station "International Space Station") and to make a joint project to travel to Mars on March 28.
- 2015: The U.S. and Russia along with the United Kingdom, France, China, Germany, the European Union and Iran sign the [Joint Comprehensive Plan of Action](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joint_Comprehensive_Plan_of_Action "Joint Comprehensive Plan of Action") to regulate Iran's nuclear program in Vienna, Austria on July 14.
- 2015: The U.S. and Russia reach an agreement on a UN resolution that would designate accountability for use of chemical weapons in Syria on August 6.
- 2015: The U.S. and Russia resume military relations to increase fighting against the [Islamic State](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islamic_State_of_Iraq_and_the_Levant "Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant") on September 18.
- 2015: Obama and Putin meet in New York to discuss ways to combat the [Islamic State](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islamic_State_of_Iraq_and_the_Levant "Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant") on September 28–29.
- 2015: The U.S. and Russia sign a deal to avoid air incidents over Syria on October 20.
- 2015: Obama and Putin have an informal bilateral meeting on the sidelines of the [G-20 Summit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2015_G-20_Antalya_summit "2015 G-20 Antalya summit") in Turkey to discuss the situation in Syria and the ramifications of the [Paris attacks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/November_2015_Paris_attacks "November 2015 Paris attacks") on November 15.
- 2015: The U.S., Russia and the United Nations hold three way talks on Syria in Geneva, Switzerland on December 11.
- 2015: The U.S. and Russia, along with the United Nations approve a resolution that supports international efforts to seek a solution to end the Syrian Civil War and provide a new government in Syria in Vienna, Austria on December 18.
- 2016 June: A debate opens inside the Republican Party on future American policy toward Russia. The presumptive presidential nominee Donald Trump suggests that US and Russia might work together in areas such as Syria. Meanwhile, on June 9, Republican leaders in Congress urged confronting Putin, alleging that he is exhibiting "burgeoning militarism" and calling for "standing up to Russian aggression and bolstering countries such as Ukraine." [^498]
- 2016 November: Donald Trump wins the US presidential election.
- 2017 April: According to Trump, US ties with Russia may be at all-time low following US missile strike on Syria.[^499]
- 2017 July: During a speech in [Warsaw](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Warsaw "Warsaw"), [Poland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Poland "Poland"), Trump warned Russia to stop its "destabilizing" actions in Ukraine and elsewhere, and its support for "hostile regimes" such as those in [Syria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Syria "Syria") and [Iran](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran "Iran"). He also urged Russia to "join the community of responsible nations".[^500]![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Vladimir_Putin_and_Donald_Trump_at_the_2017_G-20_Hamburg_Summit_%285%29.jpg/250px-Vladimir_Putin_and_Donald_Trump_at_the_2017_G-20_Hamburg_Summit_%285%29.jpg)
	U.S. president Donald Trump, Russian president Vladimir Putin, Rex Tillerson, and Sergey Lavrov at the G20 Hamburg summit, July 7, 2017
- 2017 July: Trump and Putin held a meeting for more than a two-hour period at the [G20 Summit in Hamburg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2017_G20_Hamburg_summit "2017 G20 Hamburg summit"). Secretary of State Rex Tillerson said that Trump brought up discussion about Russia's alleged interference in the 2016 US presidential election.[^501]
- 2018 July 16: [Russia–United States summit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2018_Russia%E2%80%93United_States_Summit "2018 Russia–United States Summit") between Trump and Putin took place in Helsinki, Finland. Topics of discussion included the situation in Syria, the Ukrainian crisis and nuclear arms control.[^502]
- 2021 June 16: [Russia–United States summit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2021_Russia%E2%80%93United_States_Summit "2021 Russia–United States Summit") between Biden and Putin took place in Geneva, Switzerland.
- 2021 November 19: the congressmen calling on the U.S. not to recognize Vladimir Putin as president of Russia beyond 2024. Kremlin denounced it as an attempt to meddle in its [domestic affairs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Domestic_policy "Domestic policy").[^503]
- 2022 January 24: the U.S. sent 5000-8500 troops to Eastern Europe, to assist Ukraine against a potential renewed invasion by Russia.[^504]
- 2022 February 24: [Russia invades Ukraine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_invasion_of_Ukraine "Russian invasion of Ukraine").
- 2022 December 17: Russia and the United States agreed to do a prisoner exchange. Russian arms dealer [Viktor Bout](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Viktor_Bout "Viktor Bout") and American basketball player [Brittney Griner](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brittney_Griner "Brittney Griner") were swapped.[^505]
- 2023 April 27: US imposed sanctions on Russia & Iran for wrongful detention and hostage-taking of U.S. citizens abroad.[^506]
- 2025 February 18: Riyadh meeting with Rubio, Waltz, and Witkoff on the American side, and Lavrov and Presidential Aide Yuri Ushakov on the Russian side.
- 2025 February 28: Diplomats of both sides met in [Istanbul](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Istanbul "Istanbul") on restoring regular operations at their diplomatic missions and possible continuation of direct flights between two countries.[^507] [^508]
- 2025 August 15: [President Trump](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Donald_Trump "Donald Trump") and [President Putin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Putin "Vladimir Putin") met on [August 15 in Alaska](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2025_Russia%E2%80%93United_States_Summit_in_Alaska "2025 Russia–United States Summit in Alaska") to discuss the [Ukraine-Russia war](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russo-Ukrainian_War "Russo-Ukrainian War").[^509]
- 2025 November: [President Trump](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Donald_Trump "Donald Trump") and [President Putin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Putin "Vladimir Putin") will meet in [November in Hungary](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2025_Budapest_Summit "2025 Budapest Summit") to discuss the [Ukraine-Russia war](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russo-Ukrainian_War "Russo-Ukrainian War").[^509]
- 2026 February-March: Relations between US and Russia deteriorated again after the [massive attack on Iran](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2026_Israeli%E2%80%93United_States_strikes_on_Iran "2026 Israeli–United States strikes on Iran"), which led to [Ali Khamenei's death](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Assassination_of_Ali_Khamenei "Assassination of Ali Khamenei").[^413] [^414]

## Space exploration

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Expedition_55_crew_portrait.jpg/250px-Expedition_55_crew_portrait.jpg)

The 55th expedition to the International Space Station in February 2018

[The Planetary Society](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Planetary_Society "The Planetary Society") is known to have collaborated with Russia, especially [Cosmos 1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cosmos_1 "Cosmos 1") and LIFE.

In 2014, NASA renewed a contract to ferry U.S. astronauts to the [International Space Station](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station "International Space Station") on [Soyuz rockets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soyuz_\(rocket_family\) "Soyuz (rocket family)") and [spacecraft](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soyuz_\(spacecraft\) "Soyuz (spacecraft)"). Including additional support at the Russian launch site, this contract is costing the United States $457.9 million. Along with the renewal, NASA also announced that they would be cutting some contacts with Russia after the [annexation of Crimea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Annexation_of_Crimea_by_the_Russian_Federation "Annexation of Crimea by the Russian Federation").[^510]

In June 2021, [NASA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NASA "NASA") administrator [Bill Nelson](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bill_Nelson "Bill Nelson") told CNN Business' Rachel Crane about the future of U.S.–Russian cooperation in the [International Space Station](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station "International Space Station") (ISS): "For decades, upwards now of 45 plus years \[we've cooperated with\] Russians in space, and I want that cooperation to continue. Your politics can be hitting heads on Earth, while you are cooperating" in space.[^511]

## Nuclear arms race

In 1995, a [Black Brant sounding rocket](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Black_Brant_\(rocket\) "Black Brant (rocket)") launched from the [Andøya Space Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/And%C3%B8ya_Space_Center "Andøya Space Center") caused a high alert in Russia, known as the [Norwegian rocket incident](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Norwegian_rocket_incident "Norwegian rocket incident").[^512] The Russians thought it might be a [nuclear missile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trident_\(missile\) "Trident (missile)") launched from an American submarine. The incident occurred in the post-Cold War era, where many Russians were still very suspicious of the United States and [NATO](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NATO "NATO").[^513] [^514] The Norwegian rocket incident was the first and thus far only known incident where any [nuclear-weapons state](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear-weapons_state "Nuclear-weapons state") had its nuclear briefcase activated and prepared for launching an attack.[^515]

President [Donald Trump](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Donald_Trump "Donald Trump") announced on October 20, 2018, that the U.S. would no longer consider itself bound by the 1987 [INF Treaty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intermediate-Range_Nuclear_Forces_Treaty "Intermediate-Range Nuclear Forces Treaty") 's provisions, raising nuclear tensions between the two powers.[^516] [^517] [^518] Two days later, Russian military analyst [Pavel Felgenhauer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pavel_Felgenhauer "Pavel Felgenhauer") told Deutsche Welle that the [new Cold War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Second_Cold_War "Second Cold War") would make this treaty and other Cold War-era treaties "irrelevant because they correspond to a totally different world situation." [^519] In early 2019, more than 90% of world's 13,865 [nuclear weapons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear_weapons "Nuclear weapons") were owned by Russia and the United States.[^520]

[President Putin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/President_Putin "President Putin") oversaw Russia's large-scale nuclear war exercises on October 17, 2019, where the Russian army integrated land, sea and air components of the nation's nuclear triad, nearly one year after Trump announced that the US was pulling-out of the nuclear treaty it had signed with Russia.[^521]

According to a peer-reviewed study published in the journal *[Nature Food](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nature_Food "Nature Food")* in August 2022,[^522] a full-scale nuclear war between the U.S. and Russia, which together hold more than 90% of the world's [nuclear weapons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear_weapon "Nuclear weapon"), would kill 360 million people directly and more than 5 billion indirectly from starvation during a [nuclear winter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear_winter "Nuclear winter").[^523] [^524]

After the U.S. president announced plans to resume nuclear testing in October 2025, the Russian government responded cautiously. The Kremlin stated that Russia had not conducted any nuclear tests and had not yet decided to do so. The Russian Defense Minister also indicated that if other countries resumed nuclear testing, Russia would take similar measures. These statements reflect the context of bilateral relations and nuclear competition between the two countries, highlighting the emphasis both sides place on reciprocal actions in nuclear deterrence.[^525] [^526]

## Economic ties

The U.S. Congress repealed the [Jackson–Vanik amendment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jackson%E2%80%93Vanik_amendment "Jackson–Vanik amendment") on November 16, 2012.[^527]

"Last year \[2015\] was not particularly favorable for trade between Russia and the U.S. Our overall 2015 turnover was $21 billion, a decline of 27.9 percent," said a senior Russian official in April 2016.[^528]

Reuters reported that [U.S. companies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_companies_of_the_United_States_by_state "List of companies of the United States by state") "generated more than $90 billion in revenue from Russia in 2017." [^529] According to the AALEP, "there are almost 3,000 American companies in Russia, and the U.S. is also the leader in terms of foreign companies in [Special Economic Zones](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special_Economic_Zones_in_Russia "Special Economic Zones in Russia"), with 11 projects." [^530]

A congressional report stated that "\[e\]ven before U.S.-Russia economic ties were constrained in the aftermath of Russia's annexation of Crimea in 2014 and its support for Donbas separatists, neither side had traditionally engaged in significant levels of bilateral trade and investment." [^531] The U.S. goods and services trade deficit with Russia was $11.2 billion in 2022.[^532] The total volume of trade between the two countries has fallen yearly since the [2022 Russian invasion of Ukraine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2022_Russian_invasion_of_Ukraine "2022 Russian invasion of Ukraine"), going from $36 billion in 2021, to $16.1 billion in 2022, to $5.1 billion in 2023, to $3.5 billion in 2024.[^533]

- Imports from Russia to the US
- Exports from the US to Russia

On July 28, 2025, former Russian president [Dmitry Medvedev](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dmitry_Medvedev "Dmitry Medvedev") warned that U.S. President Trump's ultimatum, threatening tariffs and sanctions if Russia didn't agree to a ceasefire within 10–12 days, could trigger a wider war.[^534] In 2026, Russia's economic envoy Kirill Dmitriev proposed an economic reintegration package which the Kremlin variously claimed to be worth $12 trillion,[^535] then $14 trillion,[^536] despite Russia's 2025 GDP being only $2.59 trillion USD.[^537] Policy analysts in America described the deal as "a losing proposition" based on Russia's economic situation and the security environment.[^538]

### Imports from Russia to the US

One major import is [enriched uranium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Enriched_uranium "Enriched uranium"). As of 2023, 24% of enriched uranium in the US is imported from Russia.[^539]

Another major import is [gasoline](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gasoline "Gasoline"), of which Russia the top provider in 2021.[^540] During the period 2003-2023 (inclusive), the low-point was December 2003.[^541]

### Exports from the US to Russia

In 2023, the [Congressional Research Service](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Congressional_Research_Service "Congressional Research Service") reported that the US was the third largest source of goods imports to Russia in 2021.[^542]

In March 2022, following the [Russian invasion of Ukraine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_invasion_of_Ukraine "Russian invasion of Ukraine"), exports from the US to Russia fell dramatically.[^543]

## Military ties

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/US_Navy_020704-N-8646S-004_Vladivostok%2C_Russia.jpg/250px-US_Navy_020704-N-8646S-004_Vladivostok%2C_Russia.jpg)

Russian and U.S. sailors honoring military personnel who perished during World War II, Vladivostok, Russia, July 4, 2002

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/2010_Moscow_Victory_Day_Parade-6.jpeg/250px-2010_Moscow_Victory_Day_Parade-6.jpeg)

An element of the 18th Infantry Regiment, representing the United States at the 2010 Victory Day military parade in Moscow.

Following the demise of the Soviet Union, the United States and Russia signed a bilateral treaty called the Strategic Arms Reduction Treaty ([START II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/START_II "START II")), signed by George H. W. Bush and Boris Yeltsin.

The United States and Russia have conducted joint military maneuvers, training and [counter-terrorist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Counter-terrorism "Counter-terrorism") exercises in [Germany](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Germany "Germany"). This was done in hopes to strengthen relations with the United States and Russia.[^544] The Russian president also proposed that the United States and Russia put a joint [missile defense system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Missile_defense "Missile defense") in [Azerbaijan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Azerbaijan "Azerbaijan"), a proposal being considered by the United States.[^545] In 2008, in response to tensions over [Georgia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georgia_\(country\) "Georgia (country)"), the United States had cancelled its most recent joint [NATO](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NATO "NATO") -Russia military exercises.

As of August 2013, the U.S. and Russia continue to hold joint [military exercises](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Military_exercise "Military exercise") like Northern Eagle (held since 2004, together with [Norway](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Norway "Norway")) [^546] and Vigilant/Watchful Eagle (with [Canada](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canada "Canada")) among others, with the aim of improving joint cooperation against [terrorism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Terrorism "Terrorism") and [piracy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Piracy_in_Somalia "Piracy in Somalia").

### Russia–NATO relations

Russia-U.S. relations are significantly influenced by the United States' leading role in [NATO](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NATO "NATO") and policies thereof. NATO and Russia agreed to cooperate on security issues at the [2002 Rome summit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2002_Rome_summit "2002 Rome summit") and had been gradually improving relations. However, due to the [expansion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Enlargement_of_NATO "Enlargement of NATO") of the alliance, the [Russian intervention in Georgia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2008_South_Ossetia_war "2008 South Ossetia war"), [Russia's war campaign against Ukraine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_military_intervention_in_Ukraine "Russian military intervention in Ukraine") and other controversies, relations have since deteriorated significantly.[^547]

In May 2015, following increased tensions with NATO, Russia closed a key [military transport corridor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NATO_logistics_in_the_Afghan_War "NATO logistics in the Afghan War") (the Northern Distribution Network), which had allowed NATO to deliver military supplies to [Afghanistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghanistan "Afghanistan") through the Russian territory.[^548] The Northern Distribution Network was established in 2010 in response to the increased risk of sending supplies through Pakistan.[^549]

A June 2016 [Levada](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Levada_Center "Levada Center") poll found that 69% of Russians think that deploying NATO troops in the [Baltic states](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Baltic_states "Baltic states") and [Poland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Poland "Poland") – former Eastern bloc countries bordering Russia – is a threat to Russia.[^550]

### Joint operations and mutual support

Russia expressed support for the United States' [war on terror](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/War_on_terror "War on terror"). Russia also agreed to provide logistic support for the United States forces in [Afghanistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghanistan "Afghanistan") to aid in anti-terrorist operations. Russia allowed U.S. and NATO forces to pass through its territory to go to Afghanistan.[^548] In 2017, the former Deputy Secretary of State [Antony Blinken](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antony_Blinken "Antony Blinken") said: "We cooperated with regard to Afghanistan, where Russia played a positive role, particularly in letting our forces and our equipment transit into and out of Afghanistan." [^551]

[^1]: In 2019, [Matthew Rosenberg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Matthew_Rosenberg "Matthew Rosenberg") wrote:

"Parts of the dossier have proved prescient. Its main assertion – that the Russian government was working to get Mr. Trump elected – was hardly an established fact when it was first laid out by Mr. Steele in June 2016. But it has since been backed up by the United States' own intelligence agencies – and Mr. Mueller's investigation. The dossier's talk of Russian efforts to cultivate some people in Mr. Trump's orbit was similarly unknown when first detailed in one of Mr. Steele's reports, but it has proved broadly accurate as well." [^276]

[^2]: **The [corroboration status of the Steele dossier's allegations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Steele_dossier#Corroboration_status_with_passage_of_time "Steele dossier") has changed with time as more information has come forth.**  
After a slow progression in the winter and spring of 2017, the FBI stopped all efforts to corroborate the dossier in May 2017 when Mueller's Special Counsel Office (SCO) took over the Russia investigation:

- "FBI had begun efforts to corroborate accusations within the dossier in the fall of 2016, an effort that progressed slowly through the winter and into the spring of 2017. When the SCO began work in May 2017, however, all those efforts ceased at FBI." [^273]<sup><span title="Page / location: 852">: 852</span></sup>
- "On standup of the SCO, the Committee lost access to all relevant information regarding FBI's efforts to verify the dossier, as it did with all information the SCO declared to touch its 'equities'." [^273]<sup><span title="Page / location: 903">: 903</span></sup>
- "FBI also made efforts to corroborate the information in the dossier memos, but the Committee found that attempt lacking in both thoroughness and rigor. The FBI pursued FISA coverage of Carter Page in October 2016, including information from the dossier, but at the time it had very little information on Steele's subsources or corroboration of Steele's information." [^273]<sup><span title="Page / location: 902">: 902</span></sup>
- "\[In May 2017,\] the SCO began its work and FBI halted efforts on the dossier." [^273]<sup><span title="Page / location: 907">: 907</span></sup>
**Corroboration status:**  
While the dossier played a significant role in initially highlighting the general friendliness between Trump and the Putin administration, the [corroboration status of specific allegations](#Corroboration_status_of_specific_allegations) is highly variable.  
Some allegations have been publicly corroborated, while some others are plausible but not specifically confirmed, and yet others are dubious in retrospect but not strictly disproven.  
The following allegations have been publicly corroborated by U.S. intelligence agencies, the [January 2017 ODNI report](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Assessing_Russian_Activities_and_Intentions_in_Recent_US_Elections "Assessing Russian Activities and Intentions in Recent US Elections"),[^274] and the [Mueller report](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mueller_report "Mueller report"):[^275]
- "The Russian government was working to get Mr. Trump elected." [^1]
- Russia sought "to cultivate people in Trump's orbit".[^1]
- Trump campaign officials and associates had [secretive contacts with Russian officials and agents](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Links_between_Trump_associates_and_Russian_officials "Links between Trump associates and Russian officials").[^277] [^278] [^279]
- Putin favored Trump over [Hillary Clinton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hillary_Clinton "Hillary Clinton").[^274] [^280]
- Putin personally ordered [^281] an "influence campaign" to harm Clinton's campaign and to "undermine public faith in the US democratic process".[^274]
- Putin ordered cyberattacks on both parties.[^274]
Some other allegations are plausible but not specifically confirmed,[^282] [^283] and some are dubious in retrospect but not strictly disproven.[^284] [^285] [^286]
